Master Thesis
Total Cost of Ownership
Date: 08/08/2014
Written by:
Isa Ilkan Duran (s083190)
Niels Hvid (s082875)
Supervisor:
Poul Andersen
Partner:
In co-operation with Man Diesel & Turbo
==PAGE:4==
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
In the past few decades a greater need for transportation is a reality on a global scale.
The globalization of the world has given the transportation branch a huge load and in
the same a great attention to the "green" solutions are a reality. With the rise of the oil
prices and rules and regulations from IMO regarding emissions, EEDI etc., the shipowners
has been squeezed from both sides. Increasing fuel prices, new legislation and a slowly
recovering of the global trade market after the crisis from 2008 has pushed the shipowners
to slow steaming solutions.
In this thesis a simulation/design programme is developed for the shipowners and
the future maritime students at DTU. The goal with this programme is to give a pre-
diction of the total cost of ownership under given parameters. This should provide the
shipowners with a tool to analyse how suitable the given ship is on a speciﬁc route and
compare alternative ships. In order to give as exact an economical estimation as possible,
several operational parameters has been included such as, but not limiting to, diﬀerent
kinds of resistance, power predictions, propeller optimization, engine fuel consumption
etc. By empirical and general formulas a quite good prediction for the ships performance
is available. In order to simplify the span of the thesis only precoded routes and ships are
available. The encoded ship types available in the programme is a chemical tanker, bulk
carrier and a container ship. The routes, which the ships can operate on, are Philadelphia
- Gothenburg, Southampton - Rio de Janeiro, San Francisco- Yokohama and Rotterdam
- Hong Kong.
This thesis deals with a simulation of the chemical tanker ship Torm Lilly in the
Philadelphia - Gothenburg route. The analysis are done for diﬀerent engine manufac-
tures, MAN Diesel & Turbo and for Wärtsilä, which will yield diﬀerent results.
The developed programme is able to calculate the resistance from the major contribu-
tors of ship operations, thereby estimating the power needed with an optimum propeller.
Furthermore the program calculates the fuel consumption, EEDI emissions and all of the
major costs parameters regarding ship operations. The program can also manage storms
in designated areas, along with power predictions of own designed propellers.
When using the programme, several diﬀerent ships can be tweaked and compared on
the or diﬀerent routes with a desired speed. This can be done in order to benchmark
diﬀerent solutions against each other in order to ﬁnd the most optimum solution with
respect to technial and economical costs.
2
==PAGE:6==
==PAGE:7==
Resumé
Gennem de seneste årtier er behovet for transport af gods gennem søvejen steget og steget.
Denne stiging i transportbehov påden globale scene har skabt grobund for ﬂere og ﬂere
skibe påverdenshavene, men samtidig ogsålagt større og større pres påat ﬁnde grønne
løsninger påde udledninger skibene har til atmosfæren. Med de evigt stigende brændstof-
priser og nye og dyrere reguleringer fra IMO, bliver skibsrederne nu presset fra ﬂere sider
af. Stigningen i brændstofomkostninger, ny forordninger og en langsomt bedrende global
økonomi efter krisen i 2008 har tvunget mig skibsreder til ogsåat slow steame.
I denne thesis er et simulerings-/design program blevet udviklet for skibsredere, men
ogsåfor fremtidige maritime studerende ved DTU. Målet med programmet er at give en
god bedømmelse af de totale omkostninger ved ejerskabet af et skib. Med dette værktøj
i hånden bør skibsreder kunne sammenligne forskellige skibe påen given rute, for at ﬁnde
det mest optimale. For at give såpræcis en vurdering, som overhovedet muligt, såsom,
men ikke begrænset til, forskellige former for modstande, eﬀekt forudsigelser, propeller
optimering, brændstof forbrug osv. Ved hjælp af empiriske og generelle formler er det
lykkedes at lave en ganske præcis estimering af ﬂere forskellige forhold under skibenes
rejser. For at simpliﬁcere denne thesis, er et antal skibe og ruter forudkodet i program-
met, dog kan det udvides med ﬂere skibe og ruter. De indkodede skibe er en kemisk
tanker, en bulk carrier og et containerskib. De valgte ruter er Philadelphia - Göteborg,
Southampton - Rio de Janeiro, San Francisco - Yokohama og Rotterdam - Hongkong.
Denne thesis har blandt andet simuleret en sejlad med den kemiske tanker Torm Lilly
påPhiladelphia - Göteborg ruten. Analysen er lavet med to forskellige hovedmotorer, en
fra MAN Diesel & Turbo og en fra Wärtsilä, hvilket giver vidt forskellige resultater.
Programmet, som er udviklet i denne thesis, er i stand til at udregne modstanden fra
alle hoved bidragsyderne under sejlads for derved at kunne estimere eﬀektbehovet som
skal anvendes med den dertil fundne energioptimerede propeller.
Derudover kan pro-
grammet ogsåberegne brændstoﬀorbruget, EEDI emissionerne og alle omkostningstunge
paramtetre mht. skibsfart. Programmet kan ogsåhåndtere simuleringen af en storm pået
angivet sted i sejladsen, samt beregne nye eﬀektkrav ud fra en propellerkarakteristik, som
brugeren af programmet gerne vil teste.
Ved anvendelsen af programmet kan ﬂere forskellige skibe benyttes, blive modiﬁceret
og sammenlignet påtværs af ﬂere forskellige ruter, alt sammen med den hastighed, som
brugeren måtte ønske. Ideen med dette er, at man skal kunne sammenligne og holde ﬂere
forskellige løsninger oppe imod hinanden for at kunne ﬁnde den mest optimale løsning,
både mht. omkostningseﬀektivitet, men ogsåmht. det tekniske aspekt.
4

Preface
This report is prepared as a master thesis for Master’s program in Engineering Design
and Applied Mechanics at the Department of Mechanical Engineering at Technical Uni-
versity of Denmark. The thesis is weighted respectively 30 ECTS points for Niels Hvid
(s082875) and 35 ECTS points for Isa Ilkan Duran (s083190) and is conducted in the
period February 3 to August 8.
Throughout the report,there is indicated who is primarily responsible for various sections,
as the thesis as a whole has been a joint co-operation between the authors.
The report forms the basis for evaluation of the students’ Master of Engineering at
DTU and therefore the report is targeted primarily supervisors, examiners and future
students within the Maritime ﬁeld of Department of Engineering.
Further the report
shall be deemed to be of interest to other people who will have an understanding in the
subject.
The report is divided into 3 main parts. The ﬁrst part of the report is a description of
how the programme is built, used and the theory behind. The second part is a simulation
section where the programme is used and the last part is a analysis of the simulation for
a life time.
The authors would like to thank the supervisor of the project Poul Andersen Asso-
ciate Professor at the Department of Mechanical Engineering for his help and guidance
in connection with the report’s technical sections and an understanding of the substance,
also for his patience and constructive guidance during the project.
Further on the authors would like to thank Jorgen Juncher Jensen, Professor DSc,
Harry Bingham, Associate Professor, and Mostafa Amini Afshar, PhD Student, all from
the Deparment of Mechanical Engineering for there support in each of their areas of
expertise.
A special thanks goes to MAN Diesel & turbo for their guidance, help and time to
meet the authors during the development of the thesis.
A special thanks also goes to Torm A/S, which has been so helpfull in providing the
authors with both main dimensions, outﬁtting, general arrangements plans and detailed
measurements from their ships with respect to operational parameters.
The chapterwise contribution to this report is as follows:
• Introduction, preface, abstract, resume: 50% Niels, 50% Isa
• Structural and Theoretical Overview of the Program: 70% Niels, 30% Isa
• Simulation Analysis: 30% Niels, 70% Isa
• Discussion: 50% Niels, 50% Isa
• Conclusion: 50% Niels, 50% Isa
The two authors has though worked together with all aspects of the litterature ﬁnd-
ings, coding and writing of the report. The teamwork has been homogeneus and very
professional. It is the authors ﬁrm believe, that even though the authors has a diﬀerence
in points in this master, the work for every point has been delivered.
Isa Ilkan Duran (s083190)
Niels Hvid (s082875)

==PAGE:21==
2
Introduction
The prediction of the total costs of ownership has always been a great challenge and
somewhat conservative. With the rising fuel costs, new environmental requirements and
a global market, a tool for estimating the total costs of ownership over a given time and
with diﬀerent options would be favourable.
Todays ﬂeet of merchant ships have all seen lower operating speeds and derating of
engines. Many of the ships sailing today have a design speed far from their operation
speed today and the seamargin is for many of the ships set conservatively to 15% or even
20%.
The purpose of this Master Thesis is to develop an analysis tool for ships in service
and partly as a tool for the design phase of new buildings. This tool is a computer pro-
gramme build in MATLAB. The structure of the programme is modular, so that each
module can be decoupled or reﬁned independently. The programme can also be expanded
to new areas by developing more and diﬀerent modules, which then are easily coupled to
the program.
In order to make the cost prediction as accurate as possible, several steps has been
taken in each of the individual modules. The Resistance Package, comprising of a wind-,
wave- and hull resistance modules, have all been extended in order to minimize assump-
tions and simpliﬁcations. Assumptions and simpliﬁcations can have a great impact on
the costs over a lifespan of 20-30 years. The Resistance package are calculating the con-
tributions from each of the modules for each time step of 1 hour on a given route or for
the number of sailings ﬁelds, which are deﬁned for the route. The sea states from all of
the world has been entered into the package, so the resistance prediction for a given route
can be as precise as possible.
After having analysed all of the contributions from diﬀerent kinds of resistance, the
costs directly related to the operations are found. These costs incur salary, fuel, write oﬀ
costs amon others. This is done in order to have an economical overview of a given ship
on a given route.
Concurring to this, the new EEDI regulations from IMO has also been taken into
account. The shipowner now has the possibility to see the amount of diﬀerent kind of
emissions on the ships sailing routes. For this purpose, a function, applying a scrubber
to the exhaust gasses, has also been incorporated into the programme.
The programme is made, so diﬀerent kinds of ships can be analyzed on diﬀerent kind
of routes with diﬀerent kind of engines. All in all to give the shipowner a better fundation
to take decisions upon.
All Nomenclature found in the following can be seen in section 1.
18
==PAGE:22==
==PAGE:23==
3
Structural and Theoretical Overview of the Program
The structure of the program created for this master thesis is of the modular kind. This
means, that the program has a backbone, where you can attach new modules to it or
detach modules. In doing so, the agility and future prospects for the program is increased
versus doing an in line program with code interveawed into each other as a single block.
Another good aspect of this way of coding the program, is that it is easier to get an
overview of the structure and the content of the program. By creating a chart over the
modules and the variables exchanged between the diﬀerent modules, a quick overview is
established. See ﬁgure 3.11.

active or not by adding or removing a % in the code:
• User Selection Box (USB)

20
==PAGE:24==
• Added Resistance due to Waves Module (ARWM)
• Propeller Optimisation Module (POM)
• Engine Consumption Module (ECM)
• Cost Analysis Module (CAM)
• EEDI Analysis Module (EAM)
Each of these modules are more detailed described in the following chapters. Joined
all together these modules proves for a strong and good comparison of diﬀerent choices of
ship outﬁtting with respect to diﬀerent or the same sailing routes. By using the modular
strategy new modules can easily be adopted to the program.
In the current conﬁguration only around 30 - 40% of the data being calculated are
actually used, the rest is not. The reason for doing so, is that during the construction
of the program, several good ideas arouse, but were out of the scope of this thesis. In
order to make future implementation easier and faster, a lot of "loose" ends has been left
in the program, so new modules are more easy to implement. This way of leaving a lot
of unused data behind is fully intentional from the authors side.
As one can see on ﬁg. 3.2 the exact path of calculations has been shown. This ﬂow
model is somewhat diﬀerent from ﬁg. 3.1, as ﬁg. 3.2 shows the exact sequence of calcu-
lations and how the diﬀerent modules are being used during the calculations.
Figure 3.2: Overview of the schematics of the program.
The next several chapters will cover the each of the modules used within this program.
The code for the entire program and all of the modules can be seen in appendix 9.8.
3.1
User Selection Box - (USB)
When executing the program, a pop up window will appear. This is the User Selection
Box or USB in short. In this window all the choices with respect to a given analysis can
be done. The USB can be seen in ﬁg. 3.3.
As one can see on ﬁg. 3.3, the USB comprises of several choices. These choises are:
• Selection of ship: In the current conﬁguration seven ships can be seen, whereof
three ships has been fully developed. These are the containership, bulk carrier and
21
==PAGE:25==
Figure 3.3: Picture of the User Selection Box
chemical tanker. The main dimensions and information with respect to each ship
can be seen in the boxes above the picture of the ship.
• Selection of route: Four major trading routes has been made and fully coded into
the programme. It is possible for all of the ships to sail on all of the routes. The
routes created are
– Philadelphia - Gothenburg
– Rotterdam - Hongkong
– San Franciso - Yokohama
– Southampton - Rio de Janeiro
• Section of loading condition: Two diﬀerent loading schemes for each ship are
possible to choose. These are the design condition, which is default, and the scant-
ling condition, which can be chosen. These conditions are precoded into the pro-
gramme, due to the use of strip theory amongst others. More about this later.
• Selection of main engine: For each ship, two main engines has been found and
applied. These main engines are either already installed in the ship or would be
suitable to ﬁt in the ship according to empirical data.
• Selection of servicespeed: There are two ways of doing the simulation. One can
either chose a ﬁxed speed or a ﬁxed time. If ﬁxed speed is selected, the program
will simulate the ship voyage with that given speed over ground, not as the speed
through water. The ship speed entered is in knots.
• Selection of crossing time: If the ﬁxed time is used instead, the program will
calculate the sailing distance and ﬁnd one ﬁxed speed, which it will use during the
entire crossing. The time entered shall be in hours.
22
==PAGE:26==
• Activation of storm: If the user wants to simulate a storm in a speciﬁc place
during the crossing, this is possible by activating the storm. Here one has to enter
the wind speed in m/s and to designate the place of the storm. The designation is
a ﬁelds number. In order to obtain the ﬁeld number, one should press the "World
Map Overview" button in order to ﬁnd the correct ﬁeld/area for the storm.
• Selection of a speciﬁed propeller: All of the ships preprogrammed comes with a
predeﬁnes propeller with respect to number of blades, pitch, area ratio and diameter.
If one want’s to test another conﬁguration of a propeller, this is possible by activing
the own propeller option and to enter the values required within the limits stated.
• Selection of scrubber: For EEDI purposes, a scrubber system has also been
implemented into the program. By activating this, the program will assume, that
the ship has been ﬁtted with a scrubber, which will have a strong inﬂuence in some
of the emissions.
After having made all of these choices, one can either press the green "Simulate" but-
ton or the red "Reset" button. By pressing "Simulate", the program will run with all of
the given input. If by some chance the input is wrong, pressing the "Reset" button will
reset all of the input and give a fresh start for the simulation.
Two of the last three buttons in the bottom either shows alot of diﬀerent variables
from the harshest area of the route and the return route created in the MWAM. Detailed
information about this window can be seen in chapter 3.14. The last button will activate
the TAM by opening a new window. See chapter 3.15 for more information about this
window and the possibilities herein.
After having run the USB, the "Reset" button has to be used before running the pro-
gram again.
23
==PAGE:27==
3.2
Main Control Module - (MCM)
The Main Control module, in short MCM, is the backbone of the program. The MCM
receives outputs from the modules and sends input around between all of the diﬀerent
modules. Furthermore it also prepares and rearranges inputs between the modules.
It is also in MCM that the diﬀerent modules can be activated or deactivated. During
the running of the program the MCM will gradually save all of the diﬀerent variable
values, which are to be used in the TAM and prepares them for application.
MCM is also handling the diﬀerent sailing routes with respect to the varying sizes
of resistance ﬁelds and keeping a consistent track on the data consistency within these
routes. The sailing routes coded into the program has been created by analysing statistics
of the worlds sailing routes, which can be seen on ﬁgure 3.5. As one can see on ﬁgure 3.4,
the routes chosen lies extremely close to those of Lloyds.
3.3
Ship Data Module - (SDM)
According to ﬁgure 3.2 the USB sends variables to the Ship Data module, in short SDM,
and get a result back. The USB asks for several parameters according to the ship selected.
The parameters are:
• Length between perpendiculars, Lpp,
for the ship
• Breadth, B, for the ship
• Displacement, ∇, for the ship
• Draught, T, for the ship
• Length over all, Loa, for the ship
• Propeller Area Ratio, AeA0, for the
ship
• The diameter of the propeller, Dmax,
for the ship
• Number of blades, z, for the ship
• The internal ship veriﬁcation number,
ship, for the programme
• The number of crewmembers, Crew,
for the ship
• The building costs, Shipprice, for the
ship
• The type of main engine, selengine, for
the ship
• The loading condition, loadcon, for
the ship
All the ships available in the program are to be found in the SDM. If additional ships
are to be added, it is to be done in this module also.
The ships, which are not fully conﬁgured, has gotten their main dimensions rewritten
with the number ﬁve everywhere for easy identiﬁcation.
24
==PAGE:28==
3.4
Sailing Time Module - (STM)
The Sailing Time Module, in short STM, handles both the ﬁxed speed scenario and the
the ﬁxed time scenario.
Within the STM the routes, which are possible to choose, has been hardcoded. The
routes has been coded in terms of waypoint, where each waypoint consist of a longitudi-
nal and latitudinal position. These waypoints in full deﬁnes the sailing route. Greater or
lesser detail with respect to the actual route can be made by either adding or reducing
the number waypoints. Between each waypoint, the assumption is, that the ship sails
in a direct line. In that sense, drifting from the forces of current or wind has not been
taking into account. Furthermore a check has been made, whether or not waypoints are
mathematically valid.
If the ﬁxed speed scenario is chosen, the program calculates the time it takes for the
ship to sail in a straight line between each waypoint. This is done by a own developed
function. The STM then returns several variables to the MCM. These are the distance,
time and speed between each waypoint.
If the ﬁxed time scenario is chosen instead, the STM ﬁrst calculates the distance be-
tween each waypoint and then summarizes it in order to estimate, what the average speed
has to be. After this many of the same calculations are carried through, as with the ﬁxed
speed part, and the STM returns the speed,time and distance betweeen each waypoint.
It is important to note, that the program calculates the same average speed for the
entire voyage, neither choice made. The average speed is built in for future development.
Future development about this module will be discussed in chapter 6.
3.5
Introduction to WRM, CWRM and ARWM
In order to do an accurate cost estimation of the operation of the ship, several parameters
come into play. One of the parameters, with the biggest contribution to fuel expenditures,
is the resistance of the ship. The simulation program created is considering three types
of resistance, which are:
• Frictional resistance against the hull, also known as calm water resistance
• Wind resistance against the area of the hull above the waterline and the superstruc-
ture
• Current in diﬀerent seas
• The added resistance due to the waves encountering the ship
These four types of resistance is, combined, responsible for almost all of the resistance
encountered by the ship during the voyage. In order to do the estimation of the total
resistance of the ship as precise as possible, fewest possible assumptions is applied within
each area of resistance. The exact construction of each of the modules is described below.
The contributions of the resistance is found for each individual speed and several
encountering angles. The speed is entered in the USB and the encountering angles used
for this program are:
• 0°
• 45°
• 90°
• 135°
• 180°
• 225°
• 270°
• 315°
25
==PAGE:29==
Where 180° is head sea and 0° is following sea.
Furthermore the ship is allowed to sail in the following directions in the program.
Only these sailing directions are allowed, which gives room for future reﬁnement in the
courses set by the ship.
• N
• NE
• E
• SE
• S
• SW
• W
• NW
The weather conditions in the Atlantic, Indian Ocean, The Paciﬁc etc. are not at all
the same with respect to wind- and wavedirections, wave spectrums etc. When looking
at ﬁgure 3.4, statistics has been made for allmost all the oceans in the world by BMT2.
BMT has measusred the wave motions over a siginiﬁcant time period for all of the world
and compiled these data into both signiﬁcant wave height and mean wave period, both
annually and monthly. These statistics has been entered into the program in order to
more precisely predict the diﬀerent weather situations in the areas where the ship is sim-
ulated to sail. Mathematically the module is set to calculate the resistance at each hour
and then add all the contributions together for the total voyage. This is done both with
respect to wind, waves, current and hull.
3.6
Wind Resistance Module - (WRM)
The Wind Resistance Module, in short WRM, is a module which calculates the resistance
due to the wind. The module gets the following inputs in order to calculate the resistance:
• Lpp
• B
• D
• T
• V
• ρAir,15
• Loa
The WRM is limited in the sense, that it can only calculate the resistance for a given
loading condition. If the loading condition changes, the whole module has to be rerun.
In order to make resistance predictions as accurate as possible, several steps has been
taken to do so. In line with the modular construction of the overall program, several of
these can be reﬁned and adjusted.
To enter exact historic data with respect to the wind and currents for all areas of the
worlds ocean into the module has proven to be very diﬃcult and to ambitious. One of
the major obstacles in this sense is, that it is hard to get access to exact and averaged
weather data for some of the most remote parts of the worlds oceans. Instead of doing
so, weather data for the investigated sailing routes has been obtained and entered into
the module. All of the weather data has been found from the source seen in reference [5].
As one can see on ﬁgure 3.4, the diﬀerent sailing routes has been inserted. The choice
of route has been done after looking at the analysis at ﬁgure 3.5, which shows how the
main trading routes are. The ﬁnal routes for the ship may diﬀer a little bit, but the cho-
sen route is deﬁnitely realistic. The wind data, which has been obtained, are presented
for each month during diﬀerent years. In order not to make the power predictions too
low, nor to high, January has been selected as the month representable for the whole year.
Figure 3.4 shows the areas for wave measurements. A diﬀerent chart is used for the
wind. A piece of this map can be seen in ﬁgure 3.6 where several of the ﬁelds of wind are

shown, but only the ﬁelds relevant to the shown routes has been entered into the support-

can be seen in appendix 9.7. The wind speeds are given in Beaufort on the pilot charts,
which by deﬁnition, is a range. The formulas, calculating the wind resistance, needs m
s
though, so the midpoint in every single Beaufort range has been found and applied. This
method is acceptable assumption from the authors. Furthermore is the ship set to sail
in only one direction in each of the ﬁelds seen on the map, as shifts in heading are not
allowed inside a ﬁeld.
Figure 3.6: A cutout of the wind and current charts.
In order to do the contributions of wind resistance from the diﬀerent corners of the
world correctly, two diﬀerent coordinate systems has to be built in to the program. The
coordinate systems are with respect to the ship and with respect to the grid of the dif-
ferent ﬁelds, the ship are sailing through. A diagram of these two coordinate systems,
with applicable values, can be seen in ﬁgure 3.7. The ship coordination system, seen to
the right, are then movable in the program coordinate system, seen to the left. The two
coordinate systems are then lying on top of eachother and the ships coordinate system
are able to move freely in the underlying system. By incorporating these two coordinate
systems into the module, the module will be able to determine what the angle is between
the ships heading and the direction of a given wind. This angle is important to determine
in order to calculate the relative wind aﬀecting the ship. This type of coordinate system
is also being used, when calculating the contribution from waves and currents during the
voyage of the ship.
The wind resistance, calculated by WRM, is done in the following manner, where ﬁrst
the relative wind, Vr, is to be found. This relative wind is found both with respect to the
diﬀerent directions of wind in each ﬁeld, but also with respect to the diﬀerent headings
the ship is sailing in each ﬁeld, as the relative wind will constantly change according to
3PhillyGott.mat, Sourio.mat, Sanyoko.mat and Rothong.mat.
28
==PAGE:32==
Figure 3.7: Schematic overview of the two coordinate systems, which are integrated into
each other.
the ships heading. The equation applied can be seen in 1.
Vr =
q
V 2 + V 2
w −2 · V · Vw · cos(θ)
(1)
where θ is the angle between the relative wind, Vr, and the ships sailing direction.
A graphical presentation of this situation can be seen in ﬁgure 3.8. These calculations
are vector based, where the windspeed and ship speed deﬁnes the lengths of each of the
vectors.
Figure 3.8: Schematic overview of the relative wind, current and wave situation.
The WRM calculates the wind resistance for all directions in each ﬁeld with the given
ship course. Each of the directions have a percentage given on the pilot charts, which can
be seen in the cut out in ﬁgure 3.6, so the ﬁnal resistance for each of the ﬁelds on the
given sailing are evaluated with respect to their representative percentage. This can be
seen in equation 2.
Rfield =
X
W% · ρAir,15 · CW · V 2
r · (AT · cos(θ)2 + AL · sin(θ)2)
(2)
29
==PAGE:33==
where W% is the percentage of the wind in the given direction.
WRM now evaluates all of the diﬀerent wind resistances from the diﬀerent directions
with respect to the ships sailing direction and with respect to where on the route the ship
is. In module terms it means, that it evaluates the wind resistance for each of the ﬁelds
it is sailing through and saves these data, which are to be used in the calculation of costs
for the ship at the given route.
The output from the WRM is the maximum-, minimum- and average resistance for
the ship going forth and back on its route. The maximum resistance are used in the
estimation of the power for the ship, but the minimum- and average resistance are shown
in the MWAM as info in order to have an idea of the wind conditions in the given area. It
is though important to note, that all the wind values used are averaged values of average
values from the pilot charts, so the shown average is somewhat lower than the actual one
and the highest resistance peaks in the given routes are aexpected to be higher at periods.
In order to have a detailed ﬁeld construction the wind areas shown in 3.6 is used. The
areas for waves (3.4) contains diﬀerent wind ﬁelds, as the pure size in kilometers diﬀers.

3.7
Calm Water Resistance Module - (CWRM)
The Calm Water Resistance Module, in short CWRM, is a module which calculates the
total calm water resistance. The module gets the following inputs in order to calculate
the resistance:
• Lpp
• B
• ∇
• T
• V
• g
• νSeawater,15
• Los
• ρSeawater,15
• Loa
• LCB
• D
• Route
The CWRM is limited in the sense, that it can only calculate the resistance for a given
31
==PAGE:35==
loading condition. If the loading condition changes, the whole module has to be rerun.
According to [4] the Calm Water Resistance coeﬃcient, CT , comprises of several com-
ponents. In this module the frictional resistance and the residuary resistance, with its
corrections, are taken into account. CWRM calculates the total calm water resistance in
the following maner.
First the CWRM calculates the Block Coeﬃcient, CB, for the ship by equation 3
CB =
∇
Lpp · B · T
(3)
After having done this, it applies equation 44 and 5 in order to calculate the Wetted
Surface Area, WSA, and the Reynolds number, Re.
WSA = 1.205 · Lpp · (CB · B + 1.7 · T)
(4)
Re =
V · Los
νSeawater,15
(5)
where V is a combination of the ship speed in the water and the contributions from
the current in the speciﬁed area5. As one can see on ﬁgure 3.6, the currents has been
indicated with direction and speed in each area. These currents are used in CWRM in
order to get the net relative current acting on the hull. This value can be either positive
or negative depending on the ships sailing direction. This value is then added to the ships
speed and thereby the true speed through the water is found.
Only the friction coeﬃcient, CF , is missing now. The friction coeﬃcient is found by
applying an empirical formula from [4] The equation can be seen in 6.
CF =
0.075
log10(Re −2)2
(6)
The next thing to ﬁnd is the residuary resistance, CR. This resistance has a signiﬁcant
contribution to the overall calm water resistance of the ship. As the residuary resistance
formulas are derived for a standard ship, acc. to Guldhammer & Harvald, some corrections
has also been implemented. The residuary resistance and its corrections are given as
CR · 103 = E + G + H + K

3.8
Added Resistance due to Waves Module - (ARWM)
The Added Resistance due to Waves Module, in short ARWM, is a module which calcu-
lates the added resistance due to waves during the ships voyage. The module has several
assumptions, which are somewhat limiting it. These assumptions and the general con-
struction of the module is thoroughly described in the following.
Assumptions:
During the calculations of the added resistance due to waves and wave spectrum some
assumptions has to be done. It is assumed that the sea is fully developed and that the sea
is considered as deep water. This assumption makes the choice of wave spectrum easier
and are acceptable due to the chosen routes (see section 3.1 selection of routes). The
analysis is done for head seas, this means that following seas are not taken into account,
only head seas, seas from 45°and seas from 90°from the stern are. The reason for this
assumption is, that head seas will give a resistance whereas the following sea will give a
"push" to the ship6. The scope of the thesis is to analyse for the predicted resistance.
There are used linear strip theory in order to estimate the added resistance.
Introduction to wave module:
In order to make a realistic description of a ship voyage from point A to B a good wave re-
sistance prediction is important. The ﬁrst thing to consider is, where does the ship travel?
The travel pattern is crucial in order to estimate the resistance from waves. All travel
patterns crosses diﬀerent areas of the world with diﬀerent weather conditions (signiﬁcant
wave height and wave period). To increase the accuracy of the module, the weather con-
ditions in each area are coded into the module, from global wave statistic (BMT)7, so its
possible to precisely consider the weather conditions in order to make a good prediction
in wave spectrum.
When the wave resistance module is generated, the very ﬁrst thing constructed is the
wave spectrum. The wave spectrum gives an overview of the wave energy in a frequency
span.
There are many diﬀerent kind of wave spectrum who ﬁts the situation wanted
to describe. The most common wave spectrum used for fully developed sea is Pierson-
Moskowitz spectrum, but there have been made modiﬁcations of this method like ISSC
and ITTC methods. The other known wave spectres is JonSwap (Joint North Sea Wave
Project) and Bretschneider spectrum. These spectres are used for more speciﬁc purposes,
like JonSwap is used if there are considered fetch limitations etc.
In this module the ISSC-method is used, because the input parameters for this method
is the signiﬁcant wave height and mean zero crossing period. With the information from
global wave statistic it is possible to make a precise description of the wave spectrum for
the whole voyage and thereby the seastate. The formula used for the Pierson-Moskowitz
wave spectrum is:
Sζ(ω) = A
ω5 exp[−B/ω4]
(15)
Where the coeﬃcients A and B is deﬁned due to the wanted version of Pierson-
Moskowitz.
In table 3.3 the values for the coeﬃcients in diﬀerent spectrum can be seen. As there
can be seen the input for the wave spectrum methods are diﬀerent8.
6Actually seas from the aft can also induce drag, but this particular area of wave ship interaction is
note fully understood and can therefore not be readily implemented
7British Maritime Technology Ltd.
8All the formulas and coeﬃcients are from [2]

Figure 3.9: ISSC-Wave spectrum
In ﬁgure 3.9 a wave spectrum is shown for a single ﬁeld (area) the ship is in. As it can
be seen there are 8 diﬀerent spectres. This is due to the direction the waves meets the
ship as described in section 3.6 illustrated in ﬁgure 3.7, but only ﬁve of them are used due
to the neglect of seas from the aft. In each ﬁeld (area) the weather contribution changes
due to the direction of the wind. Each direction has their own wave height (Hs) and
wave period (Tz), so each direction ﬁnally have there own spectre as illustrated in ﬁgure
3.9. BMT also states the percentage of seastates from each of the directions previously
deﬁned, so their weighted contributions can be calculated.
These wave spectrum is used later on to determine the added wave resistance. Beside
the hull friction resistance (calm water resistance), the added wave resistance has to be
found in order to make an accurate prediction. Back in the days the added wave resistance
was not accurate, and in the early stages of ship design a sea margin is added on to take
the added wave resistance in account. This sea margin depends on the travel pattern of
the ship. This is done due to estimation of the power prediction in a early stage of the
design, because the total resistance is used for prediction of power.
3.8.1
Added Resistance Prediction Method
In order to predict the added resistance, many diﬀerent methods can be used. It is impor-
tant to remember that it is a prediction from diﬀerent assumption. The method used in
this thesis is called the Salvesen-Method. The program used to get the added resistance
35
==PAGE:39==
and the hydrodynamical coeﬃcients for each ship section is a pre-worked Matlab script
done by Mostafa Amini Asfhar from the Department of Maritime Engineering.
The Salvesen Matlab script uses the results from I-ship9. The theory behind I-Ship is
strip theory and strip theory is used to ﬁnd the hydrodynamical coeﬃcients and transfer
functions for each of the ships in the programme. A detailed description of strip theory
is outside the scope of this thesis.
In this section a description of the I-ship programme is shown in a ﬂowchart.
Figure 3.10: Flowchart for I-ship programme
The Hull Geometry:
The very ﬁrst thing created is the hull geometry. The hull geometry can either be created
in the program or be imported. The geometry of the ship is essential in order to have
an good estimation of the transfer function and hydrostatic data for each ship section.
The full geometry is very inﬂuential on the transfer function and hydrostatic data when
applying strip theory. In ﬁgure 3.11 the geometry of a test ship is shown. As one can see
the ship is divided into to sections, where the transfer functions and hydrostatic data is
found for each section. The bigger the number of sections, the more accurate the overall
resistance prediction will be. The section places midship are not that important as the
sections at the stern and bow, because of the change of geometry. As one can see on ﬁgure
3.11, the number of sections increase in the vicinity of major hull geometry changes, e.g.
in the stern and bow. After having created the hull in I-ship, the hull is saved and ready
to move to the strip theory part.
9I-Ship is a calculation programmes for ship design, developed and used at Department of Maritime
Engineering, DTU.
36
==PAGE:40==
Figure 3.11: Deﬁnition of ship geometry
The Equilibrium Conditions:
In this section the loading conditions are chosen. The loading condition is essential due
to the resistance because the load decides the draft of the ship. In this part many loading
cases can be created, so its possible to choose between diﬀerent loading cases (design-,
scantling- and ballast condition etc.) The program calculates the total mass of the chosen
loading condition and gives the center of gravity (COG) in each direction (x,y,z). Beside
that, the software calculates the drafts at AP, midship (Lpp/2) and FP, perpendicular to
the water plane, so its possible to control the trim of the ship in a given loading condition.
In this case there are chosen zero trim in order to simplify the calculations. So the ship
are loaded in such a way that the drafts AP, midship and FP are signiﬁcantly the same.
Figure 3.12: Equilibrium Condition
Hull discretization:
When the loading condition are deﬁned, the draft (water line) is deﬁned. The software
37
==PAGE:41==
then needs to know how ﬁne the analysis should be. This is given by the hull discretization.
The hull discretization is set automatically to 15 points.
Figure 3.13: Hull discretization points
In ﬁgure 3.13 the the discretization points are shown in all of the stations. In other
words you deﬁne the number of points one wants to analyze in each section up to the
waterline. The programme then calculates the potential in each point and the hydrody-
namic coeﬃcients, hydrodynamic mass and damping, for each section.
Operational Parameters
Another part of the input to the strip theory, is the operational parameters. In this section
a range of forward speeds, maximum two, is chosen and a range of headings, maximum
seven, is chosen by giving a lowest and a highest heading angle10.
Type of Result
In this section one specify which kind of output I-Ship should give. It is possible to choose
between:
• Ship motion
• Relative motions
• Relative velocity
• Accelerations
• Sectional forces
When the wanted result type is chosen, the method of strip theory analysis is the next
thing to set. The I-Ship programme provide diﬀerent methods:
• Linear strip theory - frequency domain - 5DOF
• Quadratic strip theory - frequency domain - 2DOF
• Relative velocity
For this programme the Ship Motion and Linear Strip Theory with ﬁve degrees of
motion is utilized. When the wanted strip theory analysis is chosen the frequency domain
10If more then two forward speeds or seven heading angles are wanted for the analysis of the ship, I-Ship
has to be rerun the desired number of times.
38
==PAGE:42==
is deﬁned. It is possible to choose between wave frequency and encounter frequency. The
number of frequencies and the frequency span is also deﬁned here.
Figure 3.14: Type of Results
In ﬁgure 3.14 the inputs are shown. The type of formulation is linear, frequency do-
main. The type of strip theory is Salvesen, Tuck & Faltinsen.
Now all the needed inputs are given to I-Ship, so the strip theory can be done. The
output from the program is the transfer functions and hydrodynamic coeﬃcients for each
section, which is later used to calculate the added resistance in ARWM.
Salvesen Wave Module:
When the transfer function and hydrostatic coeﬃcients are given from I-ship for the
wanted heading and speeds, the output is used as input for the Salvesen wave script11.
11The Salvesen wave script are provided by Ph.D student Mostafa Amini Asfhar from the Department
of Maritime Engineering
39
==PAGE:43==
Figure 3.15: Flowchart for the Salvesen programme
In 3.15 the ﬂowchart for the Salvesen programme is shown. The Salvesen programme
uses three input ﬁles. The ﬁrst one is the transfer function gained by I-ship. The transfer
functions are stored in a .LST ﬁle . The second input ﬁle is the hydrodynamic coeﬃcients,
I-ship store the these coeﬃcients in a .AMD ﬁle. The last input for the Salvesen script is
a input ﬁle where following informations are deﬁned:
• stationsFile
• responseFile
• shipLength
• shipBreadth
• LBP
• cog
• nStations
• nPoints
• nfreq
• nhd
• U(1)
In the ﬁrst input parameter the station ﬁle name is given. This means that the script
needs the oﬀset ﬁle for the hull geometry (sections) in order to do the calculations. This
is provided by I-ship as an output .txt ﬁle.
The second input parameter is the response ﬁle. This ﬁle are described earlier as the
transfer function ﬁle .LST.
The other parameters are the ship dimensions as length of the ship,breadth, length
between perpendiculars, center of gravity, number of stations, number of points created,
number of frequencies, number of headings and the chosen speed(s).
Get Response:
This part of the script uses the output ﬁle from I-ship (.LST ﬁle) to rearrange/import the
transfer function so it can be used to calculate the added resistance. The I-ship output
ﬁle is for ﬁve degrees of freedom. Acc. to the formulas of Salvesen, only pitch and heave
40
==PAGE:44==
will be used in the computations of the added resistance.
HydroCoeﬀ:
This part of the programme gets the damping coeﬃcients and added mass from the I-ship
output (.AMD ﬁle). These coeﬃcients are used later to calculate the added resistance.
The I-ship data is now rewritten and are stored in the Salvesen-programme, so now
the program uses the following formula for calculating added resistance:

Figure 3.16: Output of the Salvesen script
In ﬁgure 3.16 the output of the Salvesen script is illustrated. This output is for a
single ﬁeld. The illustrated ﬁgure shows the added resistance for selected speeds and for
selected heading (head seas), the output is non dimensionalised so when the mean added
resistance has to be found according to equation 20 the output has to be dimensionalised.
When the added resistance is found for the wanted speeds and headings, several dif-
ferent Rω(ω) are computed and saved. Acc. to the structure of the program, the ships
encounters many diﬀerent sea states during its voyage due to diﬀerent encountering angles
of the waves and diﬀerent speeds. These diﬀerent Rω(ω) are then inserted into equation
20. The S(ω) function is reiterated along the ships path in the same manner as in the
WRM. By doing so the mean added resistance will be computed with a high precision.

Figure 3.17: Mean added resistance
In ﬁgure 3.17 the mean added resistance is shown for each speed in a single ﬁeld. The
wave spectrum found earlier contributes with the information of sea state and the added
resistance for the "extra" contribution of resistance for the chosen ship in a given forward
speed and heading.
So at the end there are found mean added resistance for each ﬁeld the ship passes
for a given forward speed. In order to make the programme usable an interpolation is
made so it is possible to choose between any forward speed (in range) and still ﬁnd the
contribution of added resistance due to waves.
3.8.2
Added Resistance, when SSM is activated
The steps mentioned in the previous subchapter is applicable for the normal sailing routes
encoded. The user of the program can choose to activate a storm and by doing so, the
added resistance due to waves will change, as the weather conditions changes. A full
explanation of the SSM can be found in chapter 3.9.
With respect to the seastate, the approximative SMB method, developed by Sverdrup
& Munk (1947) and Breitscneider (1952, 1958)12, is used in order to estimate it. This
method takes the wind speed as an input for calculating the sea state.
The method
incorporated into this program, has the following assumptions.
• The free wind path, fetch, which is used to generate the seastate, has the same
lenght as the ﬁeld choosen in the program. See ﬁg. 3.4 for the size of each area.
• The water depth, d, in the equation is set to 2,000 meters.
The windspeeds are almost always measured in 10 meters of height. The SMB method
is though using the windspeed in 19.5 meters of height, so the following conversion of the
windpseed is applied:

3.9
Storm Simulation Module - (SSM)
The Storm Simulation Module, in short SSM, is only activated if user actively does so.
No matter whether the SSM is activated or not, the program still runs through it.
If the SSM is activated, the user has to assign a wind value for this storm in m/s and
where on the selected route the storm is to occur according to 3.4. These values are then
used to alter the values within the WRM and ARWM.
In the WRM the pre-entered wind data are disgarded and a new matrix is built up
with the given wind from the user.
Only the placeholders in the matrix aﬀected are
overwritting with the storm values, the rest of the placeholders are ﬁlled out with the
precoded values from the other areas of the route. The program then calculates the same
resistance data as described in the WRM chapter, but it one major exception. Since it is
a storm, it is assumed that the captain will try to make headwinds in order to minimise
roliing. Therefore only headwind is calculated and it is calculated for 100% of the time
the ship is in the given ﬁeld.
In the ARWM the given seastate, which has been pre-entered, is also disregarded in
the chosen ﬁeld and the matrix is mixed in the same maner as with the wind. Instead
a new seastate is being generated for the entire ﬁeld. This is done from the windspeed
speciﬁed. More about the method can be read in chapter 3.8.2. The assumption here is
also headseas, as the captain will try to avoid damages to ship and load by sailing straight
against the waves.
3.10
Propeller Optimisation Module - (POM)
In this section the Propeller Optimisation Module, in short POM, will be described. After
the total resistance for a given ship in a given route is found, the propulsion characteristics,
propeller and main engine, has to be deﬁned.
Many diﬀerent kind of propellers are
available for diﬀerent kind of ships and use. In POM the Wageningen B series propeller
characteristics has been used, because these propellers are well documented by model
44
==PAGE:48==
experiments and is very voluminous since the number of blades (3-7), pitch ratio and area
ratio are varied.
This part of the program is a very iterative process and the variables are dependent of
each other. In order to estimate the propeller certain assumptions has to be made. Some
of the parameters has to be ﬁxed so its possible to calculate others.
For having the most eﬀective propeller the diameter has to be as large as possible, but
there is a upper limit due to the hull shape and draft. The largest propeller diameter
set is approx. 0.65 x draught, whereas the expanded area ratio (Ae/A0) and number of
blades of the propeller can be varied. When a ship type is chosen as described in section
3.1 the maximum propeller diameter, number of propeller blades and propeller area ratio
is precoded for each ship type and loaded from the SDM. The polynomials KT (thrust
coeﬃcient) and KQ (torque coeﬃcient)13, acquired from the Wageningen B-series, has
been coded into the POM.
Figure 3.18: Propeller characteristic for Wageningen B-series 4 blades Ae/A0 = 0.55
In ﬁgure 3.18 the propeller characteristic are shown for Wageningen B-series with 4
blades and area ratio 0.55. Each curve for KT , KQ and η is shown for a given pitch ratio.
The propeller characteristic can be changed into various expanded area ratio and number
of blades. The thrust coeﬃcient, KT and torque coeﬃcient, KQ is plotted along with the
propeller eﬃciency for alle of the stated pitch ratios. The formula used for the eﬃciency
is given by:

Figure 3.19: Propeller characteristic for Wageningen B-series 4 blades Ae/A0 = 0.55
In ﬁgure 3.19 the propeller characteristic is shown again, but now the needed thrust
KTn is included (eq. 30). This curve indicates the needed thrust for the propeller and
its found by the propeller load equation (eq. 29). The equation for the propeller load
(Cth) contains the thrust, propeller velocity, water density, propeller diameter. So for
each speed the needed thrust is found and a propeller load is calculated, with the found
load the needed thrust (coeﬃcient) is draw into the diagram. By this curve the needed
advance number and torque is found and the the re-iteration begins. The iteration be-
tween KTn, from the found advance number, and Cth is continued until the diﬀerence is
in revolutions pr. minute is signiﬁcantly small.
14More about this one can be seen in [9, p. 275-276]
46
==PAGE:50==
By this process, a optimum propeller coeﬃcients is found for each pitch ratio. The
program is designed to calculate for each 0.01 pitch ratio in the range of 0.5 to 1.4.
In order to estimate which pitch ratio is the optimum for a given speed, the propeller
eﬃciency equation (eq. 24) is used as the benchmark and the pitch ratio who gives the
highest eﬃciency is considered as the optimum. With the known coeﬃcient the needed
main engine torque (moment) can be found by the following equation:
Q = KQρn2D5
(31)
With the found torque the needed power are then estimated. The module calculates
the propeller coeﬃcients for each ﬁeld for a given speed and then iterates the process for a
given range of pitch ratios. After this process the highest eﬃciency decides the optimum
coeﬃcients and they are used for the power estimation.
As a note to this section, the found thrust deduction coeﬃcient by the formulas were
presented to MAN. With their empirical data in mind, they thought it were too con-
servative. Since the thrust deduction coeﬃcient has a direct impact on the eﬃciency, a
correction were given to the authors. The correction is as follows and has been imple-
mented.
ηMAN = Fac · ηB−seriesFac = 1 + 0.063 · C0.35
th
(32)
Main Engine Selection
In this section the thoughts behind selection of engine is described. As known from the
previous section,POM, the engine selection depends highly on the propeller. In a design
stage the previous described iterative method will be used to estimate the power and
revolution for the engine, but since this programme is a simulation tool an engine has
to be predeﬁned in order to simulate the reality. As one knows the needed power and
revolution will change depending of the input from the resistance modules, so therefore a
ﬁxed engine is used.
As described earlier several ship types has been selected for this programme and the only
one, with real data, is the chemical tanker Torm Lilly. The engine data for Torm Lilly
is known and precoded into the programme. For the other ships an estimation for the
main engine size is made after comparison with similar ships with same main dimensions.
These values is found in the catalogue Signiﬁcant ships15. For a usable perspective and
use of the programme two diﬀerent manufacturers of main engine is selected for each ship.
A MAN diesel engine and a corresponding Wärtsilä engine, so its possible to compare the
cost of fuel in a given route with diﬀerent engine types. The coded values are the power
for 100 % load and corresponding revolutions. By utilizing these engine data it is possible
to draw an engine diagram.
15See appendix 9.3
47
==PAGE:51==
Figure 3.20: Engine diagram for MAN diesel engine 7G50ME −9.5 −TI
In ﬁgure 3.20 the a illustration for the precoded engine data is shown as an engine
diagram. The engine is chosen in the User Selection Box, USB, described in section 3.1.
So its possible to run a simulation with diﬀerent speeds and then "plug" them into the
diagram and analyse how the engine will perform in a given route. This analysis will be
described further in the simulation section.
Figure 3.20 illustrates the engine layout diagram, this diagram is created with the
knowledge of needed eﬀect (power) and propeller revolutions, and with this diagram the
engine type and number of cylinders is decided. The layout diagram shows a combined
area of number of revolution and eﬀect, where its "free choice" to select the optimum
combination for a given ship with a expected operating condition. As there can be seen
in the layout diagram it is limited by two lines, for the upper and lower MEP (Mean
Eﬀective Pressure), and two line for limited number of revolutions. With these limitations
four corners is obtained for the layout area of the engine, namelyL1, L2, L3, and L416, and
the engine should normally operate within this area during its voyages.
3.11
Engine Consumption Module - (ECM)
The Engine Consumption Module, in short ECM, is a module which calculates the con-
sumption of heavy fuel oil and lubrication oil for the main engine and the auxiliary engines.
The diﬀerent values of heavy fuel oil and lubrication oil consumption are then returned
to the MCM, which sends it to the next module in line.
3.11.1
Main Engine
In order to do the most precise estimation of heavy fuel oil consumption, a speciﬁc engine
has to be speciﬁed. For each of the ships active in the program, to engines has been
coded in from two of the biggest marine engine constructors. The program are looking
at engines from Man Diesel & Turbo and Wärtsilä. With respect to Torm Lilly, which is
the chemical tanker, the authors has received blueprints of the actual engine and only a
counterchoice had to be found. For the two other ships complete engine selections were
necessary. A quick look at the two random engines, see ﬁgure 3.21 and 3.22, shows some
very diﬀerent BSFC 17 curves and more the reason to have two engine manufacturers
16See more in [6] chapter 3.2
17Brake Speciﬁc Fuel Consumption
48
==PAGE:52==
coded into the module.
Figure 3.21: BSFC for the MAN 7G50ME-C9.5-TII.
Figure 3.22: BSFC for the Wärtsilä 7RT-ﬂex50-B.
When looking through theBFSC curves from the two manufacturers, one quickly re-
alilzes, that they are nothing alike and that they are quite diﬀerent almost everywhere on
the power curve. Since the user of the program are able to insert varying speeds into the
program and thereby will receive varying power outputs required for diﬀerent voyages, a
simple listing of table values will not be adequate. The BFSC curves has therefore been
approximated with a polynomium to the power of six in order to get a good estimation.
The data, which these polyniums are ﬁtted to, can be seen in appendix 9.6.
With the polynomium programmed into MatLab, along with the lubrication oil con-
sumption, gives the consumed amount of heavy fuel oil and lubrication oil with respect
49
==PAGE:53==
to both the number of ﬁelds, the voyages has been subdivided into, but also with respect
to each hour, the engine is running.
Implementing a new engine into the program is relatively straightforward. The param-
eters needed is a polynium for the for fuel consumption and the consumption of lubrication
oil when running. These two components can be incorporated into the program within
30 minutes. Slight changes also has to be made to the USB and the CAM, where the new
engine selection has to be built in.
3.11.2
Auxiliary Engines
The three ships, which has been entered into the program, are somewhat alike with re-
spect to main dimensions and the auxiliary power needed. There are no cooling ships,
LNG tankers or containerships with 4,000 or more reefers. It has therefore, by simplicity
reasons, been assumed that the outﬁtting of auxiliary are so close to each other, that it
is practically the same.
The authors has been in dialogue with the danish shipping company Torm, which pro-
vided a lot of useful information for Torm Lilly, the chemical tanker in the program. Some
of this information is live measurements from a normal voyage the ship has undertaken.
According to these measurements, the ship uses between 70 - 100 kg of marine gas oil an
hour. The peaks around 100 are few and the medium value has been calculated to be 77
kg an hour. This consumption of marine gas oil is applied to all three ships every hour
during the entire voyage. It has not been possible to measure the amount of lubrication
oil consumed by these engines.
3.12
Cost Analysis Module - (CAM)
The Cost Analysis Module, in short CAM, is a module which all aspects of costs with
respect to a given voyage. In order to do so, the CAM receives the following information
from the MCM:
• Number of crew on the ship and the
distribution of low and high payed
crew members
• The shipprice of the given ship
• The BSFC for the voyage, both fourth
and back, and both with respect to
the subdivision of ﬁelds and pr. hour
• The amount of lubrication oil con-
sumed during the voyage
• The ship speed
• The time outputs from the STM
• The ship selected by the user
• The engine selected by the user
All of the data up until now has been calculated with a minimum amount of assump-
tions and conservatism in order to make the costs estimation of the ship as precise as
possible. The same approach will also be applied here.
Within the CAM the following cost assumptions has been set:
• Price of lubrication oil is 10 $US pr. liter
• Price of Heavy Fuel Oil is set to 620 $US pr. metric ton18
• Price of Marine Diesel Oil is set to 899 $US pr. metric ton19
18Data obtained from bunkerworld.com on the 27th of June, 2014
19Data obtained from bunkerworld.com on the 1st of July, 2014
50
==PAGE:54==
• The high salary crew receives 40 $US an hour
• The low salay crew receives 20 $US an hour
• Food costs is set op 7 $US pr. crewmember pr. day
• Miscelleaneus costs is set as an linear function of the number of crew. This is set to
13 $US pr. day. This includes light ship maintenance, crew beneﬁts etc.
• Maintenance costs for a Man Diesel & Turbo engine is set to 43 $US pr. operation
hour20.
• According to Man Diesel & Turbo, Wärtsilä maintenance costs are somewhat 10 %
higher. This has also been incorporated into the CAM 21.
• A salary factor for the crew has been incorporated in order to take into account the
normal patterns for seafarers, where they are part time out, part time home, with
pay.
• Writeoﬀcosts of the ships purchasing value is done over a 30 year period.
The CAM then calculates the following parameters in two diﬀerent ﬁles, as it is to
return the cost calculations both with respect to the costs in each ﬁeld on the sailing
route and with respect to total time of the voyage, where the timestep is pr. hour.
• Total costs, where all the costs has been summarized
• The fuel costs for the main engine
• The fuel costs for the auxiliary engines
• The maintenance costs for the main engine
• The size of salaries
• The size of the miscelleaneus costs
• The writeoﬀcosts
All of these calculated data are then returned to the MCM for further processing.
3.13
EEDI Analysis Module - (EAM)
The EEDI Analysis Module, in short EAM, is a module which looks into the EEDI aspects
for the main engine of a given voyage.
This module has been strongly inspired after
talkings with Man Diesel & turbo. In short, engine manufacturers complies directly to
the current Tier requirements in force, no better. Complying to lower emission standards
will only give a poorer performance of the main engine and thereby each horsepower will
be more expensive, as the engine costs will either stay the same or increase slightly. With
this statement in mind, the authors has used the statistical material from [8] in order to
determine the diﬀerent emissions from the main engine. According to [8], the table for
slow and medium speed engines, which respectively is the main and auxiliary engines,
are:
The numbers shown above are based on extensive empirical material and is found to
be reasonable. Due to future regulations with respect to low sulfur areas and the Tier

III requirements, several ships are testing scrubbers, to lower harmful emissions. This
option has also been incorporated into the EAM. According to appendix 9.4, applying a
scrubber will remove almost all of the sulphur from the exhaust gas and up to 80 % of
the particulates. In the EAM the values used, when the user applies the scrubber to the
voyage, are the removal of 98 % of the sulphur and 78 % of the particulates.
With these numbers ready, the EAM receives the following information from the
MCM:
• The Heavy Fuel Oil consumed pr. hour and pr. ﬁeld on the given voyage
• The Marine Diesel Oil consumed pr. hour and pr. ﬁeld on the given voyage
• The time it takes for to sail the entire voyage and the time it takes to sail in each
of the ﬁelds
• Whether or not the ship is ﬁtted with a scrubber
With all of these inputs given the EAM returns EEDI emission calculations to the
MCM. The EAM returns the overall emissions for each component, but also returns
the results for each component in each ﬁeld and pr. hour for each of the engine types
separately.
3.14
Maximum Weather Analysis Module - (MWAM)
The Maximum Weather Analysis Module, in short MWAM, is a module which gives a
quick, but accurate overview for the worst part of the voyage. The module is more appli-
cable to design cases, but is also also important for analysis purposes.
A presentation of the MWAM can be seen in ﬁgure 3.23. The diﬀerent important has
been grouped into diﬀerent categories.
52
==PAGE:56==
Figure 3.23: MWAM representation
The MWAM has the ability to show ﬁrst of all, all of the main dimensions for the ship,
so this important data is easily accessible. It also shows the desired speed over ground,
along with the time the voyage takes and the distance the ship has to travel in nautical
miles.
The next segment of boxes shows the results for the propeller and the engine. The
propeller listing includes its calculated wake fraction and thrust deduction coeﬃcient.
The needed engine torque and power is also shown, along with the predeﬁned propeller
dimensions, unless an own propeller has been entered before running the programme.
At the bottom of the window is some important resistance data. These data shows
the maximum and minimum wind resistance. If the minimum wind resistance is nega-
tive, then it means, that the ship has some tailwind during the crossing. The average
wind resistance is also shown, along with the overall resistance. Futhermore the calm wa-
ter resistance and the minimum and maximum added resistance due to waves can be seen.
3.15
Total Analysis Module - (TAM)
The Total Analysis Module, in short TAM, is a module which gives a graphical repre-
sentation of all of the calculated data with respect to the simulation. The TAM can be
accessed through the USB. A presentation of the TAM can be seen in ﬁgure 3.24

return route.
• General data
• Engine Analysis
• Cost Analysis
• Fuel Analysis
• EEDI Analysis
Furthermore a group of "General Overview" data has been made, so the user have a
general idea of the weather conditions on the route and the geographical position of the
route. An detailed overview of all of the buttons will be left out, as the labels for each
button is selfexplanatory. On a general scale it can be said, that all of the plots works in
to ways. The data are either shown against the time it takes for the crossing or by the
sailing ﬁelds it is going throug. A detailed overview of the sailing ﬁelds can be seen in
table 3.2 or by looking at appendix 9.7, where the geographical place is marked.

Simulation Analysis
After having looked through all the technical and theoretical thoughts of the program,
this sections main goal is to apply and use the program on a given ship, with a speciﬁc
route in mind. The ship chosen is the Torm Lilly and it is set to sail on the Philadelphia
- Gothenburg route and back.
During the analysis several parameters will be looked into and for some of these, an
optimum solution will be investigated. The analysis covers everything from resistance,
over propulsion to the costs associated with the chosen ship and route.

In table 4.4 and 4.5 the mean percentage of the single resistances are shown. As one
can see the dominating resistance type are the friction, also known as calm water resis-
tance. Next after this one is the added resistance due to waves. The contribution of the
wind resistance is signiﬁcantly smaller. For a increasing forward speed the friction resis-
tance will dominate heavily. This analysis is especially relevant in order to understand
which kind of resistance the ship will meet during the voyage. It is possible to conclude
that the shape of the ship hull plays a huge role in order to minimize the resistance, e.g.
a more hydrodynamically correct design will minimize the frictional resistance alot. The
analysed ship is a chemical tanker ship which is known for having a great volume, which
is very much box shaped as indicated by its block coeﬃcient of 0.8244, which means the
frictional resistance is expected to be relatively high. A container ship however has a more
slim hull shape (v-shape hull) and will be expected to have a signiﬁcantly less frictional
resistance.
In order to get an graphical overview of the contributions of each single resistance com-
ponent for the diﬀerent speeds chosen, ﬁgure 4.10 - 4.17 gives that. The ﬁgures both show
the diﬀerences whether you are going from the West to the East or the other way around.
Moreover they show the diﬀerences in magnitude between the diﬀerent speeds, but also
relative magnitude within each speed.
The bigger the speed, the lessor the inﬂuence
from the wind generated resistance and the added resistance due to waves. This internal
strenght between the diﬀerent contributions is of particular interest, when analysing the
ship. If the ship were to have a new operational proﬁle with a smaller speed than the 12
kn., then it would be beneﬁciary to have an idea were to optimize. Should the hull shape
in front, including the bulbous bow be redesigned or should something be done to the
aero dynamical design of the ship.

Propeller Eﬃciency Analysis
In this section the propeller eﬃciency is analysed. The propeller eﬃciency indicates
how much work the propeller delivers to the water by what eﬃciency and thereby the
propulsion of the ship under the given circumstances. As described earlier the chosen
propeller characteristics for this propeller is a 4 bladed, ﬁxed pitch, single propeller with
an expanded area of 0.58 a pitch ratio of 0.7, the diameter of the propeller is 6 m. In
order to calculate the propeller eﬃciency for the ship in the chosen route equation 24 is
used with the Wageningen B- propeller series.
Philadelphia - Gothenburg
Speed [knots]
6
8
10
12
Mean Propeller Eﬃciency
0.37
0.41
0.43
0.44
Gothenburg -Philadelphia
Speed [knots]
6
8
10
12
Mean Propeller Eﬃciency
0.33
0.36
0.37
0.38
Table 4.7: Mean propeller eﬃciency for each forward speed and route
In table 4.7 the mean propeller eﬃciency can be seen for each chosen forward speed
and route. As one can see the propeller eﬃciency is increased with the speed, but the
eﬃciency is still very low. This can be due to the chosen pitch ratio or expanded area.
There is a dilemma when the expanded area is decided, because in one way this area
will be chosen as low as possible due to the friction of the surface against the water, but
in the other way the area has a limitation to how low it can physically be. If the load
of the blades for each area unit is to high,the blades are subjected to a rapid change of
pressure that cause the formation of cavities where the pressure is relatively low. When
subjected to higher pressure, the voids implode and can generate an intense shock wave
and fragmentation of the surface. This phenomenon is known as cavitation. If this occurs,
then the lift forces of the blade will not be as expected the resistance of each of the single
blades will increase and a lot of energy will be wasted. This will result in a lowering of the
propeller eﬃciency, which is not desired. The cavitation can be avoided by distributing
the force of the blades over a greater area, in other words increasing the expanded area
ratio.
The overview of the propeller eﬃciency for each ﬁeld and speed for both sailing ways
can be seen in ﬁgure 4.34 - 4.41.

4.2
Storm Analysis
In order to give a realistic prediction of what might happen in a given route, a storm
module has been built-in as described in section 3.9. In this section a demonstration of
this model will be illustrated. For having a useful and comparable results the used ship
and route data are the same as in section 4.1 and the theory behind the storm module
can be seen in section 3.8.2
The statistical weather prediction for Philadelphia-Gothenburg is already known as
showed in ﬁgure 4.1, thereby the roughest area under the voyage is known, so the possi-
bility of this area will have storm conditions at some time, are higher. The main question
here is, what if this area contains a storm given weather condition, how will this eﬀect
the ship? To answer that the storm condition has to be analysed.
The wind speeds in a given area decides how bad the weather conditions are, and in
order to rank the conditions the Beaufort scale has been used (table 3.1). A wind speed
of 25 m/s will give storm conditions (Beaufort 10) and this wind speed is used for the
illustration purpose. The storm condition is applied in area 16 which is the roughest area
in the Philadelphia-Gothenburg route (ﬁg: 4.1. There are assumed that the ship has a
service speed of 12 knots and will maintain this forward speed during the voyage22.) The
argumentation for the chosen service speed is that, is a general service for a chemical
tanker.
4.2.1
Storm eﬀect on the ships total resistance
In this section the storm eﬀect will be illustrated by the given assumptions and values
described in section 4.2. The program has now been run with a created storm in area
16 in Philadelphia - Gothenburg route. The resistance analysis will be present for both
cases (with and without storm) for comparison.
22The authors are aware of the impossibility of this fact, but the program is not able to vary the speed
to the power installed yet
70
==PAGE:74==
Figure 4.50: Total resistance for Philadelphia -
Gothenburg with storm
Figure 4.51: Total resistance for Gothenburg -
Philadelphia with storm
Figure 4.52: Total resistance for Philadelphia -
Gothenburg without storm
Figure 4.53: Total resistance for Gothenburg -
Philadelphia without storm
As there can be seen in ﬁgure 4.50 and 4.51 the resistance in sailing ﬁeld 8-13 area
(Wave area 16) is signiﬁcantly higher compared to the resistance without storm in ﬁgure
4.52 and 4.53, this is due to the applied storm as described.
One might also wonder, why the total resistance for the case without storm (ﬁg: 4.52
and 4.52) not highest in area 16 both ways? As it can be seen by the ﬁgures for the case
without storm, the total resistances is highest in diﬀerent areas across the voyage for each
of the directions across the North Atlantic. This is simply due to the inﬂuence of current
in the given areas. The selection of the roughest area are only done for the highest mean
wave hight and mean wave period (table 4.1).

an abnormal increase in the total resistance, but the goal of this simulation was to analyse
how the storm will eﬀect the ships total resistance in order to maintain a service speed
of 12 knots, so this increase is therefore somwhat expected. It also shows how rough the
weather can be and the magnitude of the forces at play. For further investigation of the
storm eﬀect on Torm Lilly, the change in the contribution of the every single resistance
component is quite relevant.
4.2.2
Storm eﬀect on the ships total resistance
In this section a further analysis on change in single contribution of resistance due to
storm is examined. In ﬁgure 4.54 and 4.55 the single contributions of the single resis-
tances is shown. As it can be seen there are huge increase of the area where the storm
a applied. In ﬁgure 4.56 and 4.57 the contribution of single resistance without storm is
shown. In order to have a deeper understanding of the eﬀect of the storm a diﬀerence for
each resistance type is calculated.
In table 4.10 the changes in calm water resistance is shown. As there can be seen
there is no changes because the calm water resistance does not depend on the wind speed
or weather conditions, but mainly to the hull shape and the forward speed (inclusive
the current inﬂuence). The columns in the table illustrates sailing ﬁelds, the calm water
resistance for Philadelphia-Gothenburg direction and Gothenburg-Philadelphia direction
for storm and without storm, and the last part is the comparison.
In table 4.11 the changes in wind resistance is shown. As there can be seen the diﬀer-
ence in percentage is huge, but if the numbers are analysed in each direction one will see
that in the storm case, there will be maximum 37 kN wind resistance. This is simply due
to the assumption made in section 3.9, that the ship is sailing head sea. The surface area
where the wind force will act are minimal, but still compared with the statistical weather
prediction the storm case has a big increase, approx. 1,700% in Philadelphia-Gothenburg
direction and approx. 400 % in Gothenburg-Philadelphia direction.
In table 4.12 the change in added wave resistance is shown. For the Philadelphia-
Gothenburg direction the diﬀerence for with and without storm is signiﬁcant. The value
increases from 200 kN to 5,796 kN which is equivalent to a increase of 2,800%. For the
72
==PAGE:76==
Gothenburg-Philadelphia direction the same trend can be seen. The added wave resistance
without storm is about 140 kN and with storm the value increase to approx. 5,330 kN,
which is equivalent to 3,600%. The reason to the large diﬀerence in the added resistance
in waves is due to the new wave height and mean period which are found with the use
of the SMB method by equation 22 and 23. The wave height and mean wave period
was found by statistical prediction in global wave statistics, but the SMB - method uses
the given wind speed to calculate the new wave height and mean wave period (further
description on the SMB method can be seen in section 3.8.2)

Philadelphia with storm
Figure 4.56: Single resistance for Philadelphia -
Gothenburg without storm

In table 4.13 and 4.14 the calculated results are shown for Torm Lilly on both of its
routes. As it can be seen there are results for the maximum area and a mean result
for the whole voyage. In order to make an estimation of how the chosen main engine
will handle the voyage, these result are plotted with the engine diagram for both engine
types (MAN main engine and the Wärtsilä main engine). This is done in the next chapter.
Furthermore it can be seen from the tables, that the 14 knots has been used for the
Philadelphia - Gothenburg trip. This is due to the fact, that the engine can operate in
this region, whereas if the ship sails the other way around, the power requirements will
be bigger then both of the main engines in the program can deliver. From the tables it is
also seen, that the standard deviation regarding the calculated mean of both the power,
but also the revolutions, are quite high. The revolutions lies around 7 - 11%, whereas the
power 14 - 33%. This big of a gap in the standard deviation indicates to the reader, that
the consistency in the data is not very good and that the data are quite widespread. This
is important to have in mind when reading the rest of the simulation analysis.
MAN Main Engine Analysis
The corresponding needed power and engine revolutions are plotted into the engine
layout diagram for each sailing direction. In ﬁgure 4.58 and 4.59 the engine layout diagram
for the roughest area is shown. With the installed MAN engine the ship will reach the
maximum revolutions before the maximum eﬀect. This means that the engine will not
be able to reach the maximum eﬀect and thereby the maximum speed. With the given
circumstances (table 4.13 and 4.14) the ship will be able to sail approx. 8.5 knots on the
Philadelphia - Gothenburg route (ﬁg. 4.58) and approx. 8.0 knots for the Gothenburg
- Philadelphia route, (ﬁg. 4.59). As mentioned earlier these layout diagrams are for the
75
==PAGE:79==
roughest area for both of the routes. A speed of 8.0 knots in this area will be suﬃcient
in order to sustain manoeuvrability. For having a general overview of how the engine
perform for the whole voyage, the mean values has been calculated and used along with
its corresponding standard deviations, so its possible to see how the values varies. In
ﬁgure 4.60 and 4.61 the layout diagram with the propeller curve is shown. Torm Lilly
will be able to sail approx. 11.5 knots with the given MAN engine in from Philadelphia
to Gothenburg, which is decreased to approx. 9.5 knots on the route from Gothenburg to
Philadelphia, which is not optimal at all.
In ﬁgure 4.60 and 4.61 the standard deviations are also shown as pointers sticking out
from the speed points. As expected the standard deviation grows with growing speed and
seems relatively large. These deviations are quite large and indicates that the accuracy
of the calculated speeds are not that good. Everywhere within the lines is it possible for
the speed point to lie. This does though not change the fact that the MAN engine is not
going to be able to use more than half of its power during the crossings .
Figure 4.58: Engine diagram for MAN diesel engine 7G50ME −C9.5 −TII when sailing
Philadelphia - Gothenburg in the roughest area.
Figure 4.59: Engine diagram for MAN diesel engine 7G50ME −C9.5 −TII when sailing
Gothenburg - Philadelphia in the roughest area.
