Wärtsilä Main Engine Analysis
The same analysis, as done above, is done for the Wärtsilä main engine. This engine
is actually the one which is installed on Torm Lilly. With the found needed power and
propeller revolutions showed in table 4.13 and 4.14 for each way over the North Atlantic
the layout diagram for the roughest area is shown in ﬁgure 4.62 and 4.63. As one can
see the maximum forward speed the engine can produce will be approx. 11.0 knots for
trip Philadelphia - Gothenburg and approx. 10.5 knots for the Gothenburg - Philadelphia
trip. From the ﬁgures it is possible to see, that the propeller curve leaves the engine area
almost in the top of the righthand side of the diagram. This means, that even though the
resulting speed diﬀers for the two directions, then all of the power produced by the engine
is used. The fact, that the program estimates the engine to ﬁt so well in the roughest
area is nice to see and somewhat validates the program and the ﬁxed propeller set by the
authors to this analysis. In ﬁgure 4.64 and 4.65 the same analysis can be seen, although it
is for the mean resistance values calculated across both directions over the North Atlantic.
As one can see the engine can produce almost 14.0 knots when sailing from Philadelphia
to Gothenburg and almost 12.0 knots when sailing the other way. Again the maximum
revolution is reached within the maximum power of the engine. As a note to this, this
could show some weaknesses in the program, as it is to the authors best knowledge not
possible for such a ship to sail 14.0 knots. This conclusion is partly due to the authors
knowledge, but also due to talks with Man Diesel & Turbo about Torm Lilly.
In ﬁgure 4.64 and 4.65 the standard deviations are also shown as pointers sticking
out from the speedpoints. As expected the standard deviation grows with growing speed
and seems relatively large. This could indicate some resistance data inconsistency of the
program, but not enough to disregard the results.

phia - Gothenburg in the roughest area

4.4
Fuel Consumption Analysis
4.4.1
Main Engine
After having analysed the entire voyage of Torm Lilly from Philadelphia to Gothenburg
and back, several outputs has been found. As seen in the previous section, the power re-
79
==PAGE:83==
quirements both with respect to the subdivided ﬁelds of the voyage, but also with respect
to each hour, has been calculated. With the power requirements set, it is now possibly
to do a comparison of the two chosen main engines for Torm Lilly, namely the originally
ﬁtted Wärtsilä 7RT-ﬂex50-B and the MAN 7G50ME-C9.5-TII. The speciﬁc engine data
for both engines can be seen in appendix 9.6.
The important thing to analyse here, is the Brake Speciﬁc Fuel Consumption, in short
BFSC. The BFSC gives an exact reading of the instantaneous amount of consumed fuel
used. As seen on ﬁgure 4.66 and 4.67, the two BFSC curves for each of the engines are
not quite similar.
Figure 4.66: BSFC for the MAN 7G50ME-C9.5-TII.
Figure 4.67: BSFC for the Wärtsilä 7RT-ﬂex50-B.
Torm Lilly is originally ﬁtted with the Wärtsilä 7RT-ﬂex50-B engine, so it could be of
great interest to analyse whether or not the MAN 7G50ME-C9.5-TII could be a suitable
80
==PAGE:84==
change. From ﬁgure 4.66 and 4.67 it is evident, that the operation area of the engine has
a major inﬂuence of the fuel costs. The engine from MAN is only really competitive from
around 8,000 kW and upwards. According to empirical data, the servicespeed for Torm
Lilly should be around 12.0 knots, so we have set it to exactly 12.0 knots and done all of
the calculations with this maximum speed in mind. Furthermore the loading condition
is set to the design condition and not the scantling condition. In ﬁgure 4.68 to 4.75, the
power required for the four diﬀerent sailing routes can be seen.
Figure 4.68: Philadelphia - Gothenburg
Figure 4.69: Gothenburg - Philadelphia
Figure 4.70: Rotterdam - Hongkong
Figure 4.71: Hongkong - Rotterdam

From the ﬁgures it is clearly seen that the power requirements diﬀers quite much
across the world. It could therefore be of interest to see what the average power needed
in each sailing route is. These numbers can be seen in table 4.15.

As seen in table 4.15, the average power values lies closely around the interesting
change in performance for both of the main engines. The numbers also indicate, that
there are quite a signiﬁcant diﬀerence in the power required for operating on these routes.
For now it is still interesting to compare the two engines against eachother, but it has to
be done on a more detailed level. This level is to look at the exact fuel consumed on each
route, as the power requirements calculations do not give a clear picture of which engine
is the most fuel economical and favorable.
An indirect and more interesting way of seeing this is looking at the fuel costs for
the diﬀerent routes accoring to table 4.16. The fuel price is the same, but the diﬀerent
amounts of burned heavy fuel oil is directly readable in the heavy fuel oil costs for each
route.
As seen in 4.16 the diﬀerences in heavy fuel oil costs, and inherently the fuel oil con-
sumption, is great. The diﬀerence is constantly around 70% more fuel burned with the
MAN main engine, instead of the Wärtsilä main engine. But how can the diﬀerence be-

Table 4.16: Fuel costs for all of the routes with Torm Lilly at 12.0 knots.
tween these two almost identical engines be so great.
To answer this, there is not one single reason, but rather a number of reasons. The
ﬁrst reason to mention, is the very diﬀerent BFSC curves for both of the engines. The
curves are only somewhat identical from 8,000 kW and then to their limit. Underneath
this point, the MAN engine uses signiﬁcantly more heavy fuel oil then the Wärtsilä. Hav-
ing this in mind, when looking at table 4.15, it makes sense. When studying the sailing
route more narrowly directly in the tables generated from the program, one will also re-
alize, that the engine is not at all required to use all of its power for the entire voyage,
rather on the contrary. Since the most of the diﬀerent voyages has quite some time in the
area beneath the 8,000 kW break point, the MAN engine will consume signiﬁcantly more
fuel most of the time.
Another reason, or inaccurancy, is the powercurves. Due to a limitation in the pro-
gramme, it is currently not possible for the program to have an upper limit with respect
to the power required in the diﬀerent ﬁelds of the trip. The program is dumb in the
sense, that it calculates what the power requirements are to withhold the entered speed,
not looking into whether or not the machinery can deliver it. Since this is the case, the
6th order polynomial should also be applied with care outside its intended range, which
is the speciﬁed power range from the individual manufacturers. The programme has in-
stead gotten a relief valve, which means, that outside this area, a ﬁxed amount of fuel
pr. kW is used. This value lies between 168 - 171
g
kWh. This rather primitive assumption
is acceptable, although it does introduce quite some inaccurancy into the ﬁnal result, as
the speed through water has an signiﬁcant contribution by several powers to the ﬁnal
resistance and thereby the fuel consumption.
Another reason is, that the ship has been simulated with a given propeller. This is
not quite the right to do it, but with a lack of data, the propeller chosen is the best guess
possible from the authors side. The authors are well aware of the fact, that propellers
and engines are designed and dimensionalised closely together, as each propeller ﬁts a
speciﬁc engine and not several diﬀerent engines. It is therefor rather diﬃcult to estimate
the exact inaccurancy on this point, but it has been tried to be levelled by applying the
same propeller to each of the engines.
4.4.2
Auxiliary Engine
The precise outﬁtting of Torms auxiliary enginges are known, as the shipowner company
TORM has handed over the data to the authors. For the moment, the program does not
have a module doing optimisation analysis of the auxiliary engines. This is partly due to
83
==PAGE:87==
missing and hard to ﬁnd information on how much power the diﬀerent ships, activated in
the program, needs on diﬀerent times of their voyages.
Some of the data obtained from TORM does though indicate exactly how much fuel
the auxiliary engines are consuming. The data set is realtively big and a good estimation
is possible to obtain from this. It is also this estimation, which is 77
kg
hour, that has been
used in the programme, along with the fact that the auxiliary engines runs all of the
sailing time. The authors has not incorporated an axle generator to support and relieve
the auxiliary engines.
4.5

In the recent decades, awareness of the impact of our acitvites on the environment has
gained an increasingly more momentum. It is now of greater and greater importance and
this has for example resulted in the Tier requirements.
Previously in this thesis the fuel consumption for each of the routes, with diﬀerent
main engines, were looked into. From these data it is possible to obtain several important
EEDI emission data, to get an understanding of the magnitude of emissions into the
atmosphere. In table 4.17 and 4.18 a sum of the emisssions from the routes are shown for
each of the engines.

amount of pollution, that the atmosphere could be saved from.
4.6
Cost Analysis
The cost analysis is done with Torm Lilly as the chosen ship. Given the data represented
in section 4.4 and 4.5, the diﬀerences in fuel consumption between the MAN and Wärtsilä
are siginiﬁcant. It is therefore expected, that the fuel costs concerning Heavy Fuel Oil
will be signiﬁcantly larger for the MAN engine, than the Wärtsilä counterpart.
In the following only one route will be commented and analysed, as most of the con-
clusions will be similar for the other routes. This is primarily due to the relatively small
diﬀerence in parameters the costs are separated into. The program, as for now, do not
take harbour fees, towing costs, support ship prices etc. into account. Nor does it take
the diﬀerences in fuel prices into account, as these diﬀer depending on which part of the
world you are refuelling in. The program does not take local valutadiﬀerences against the
dollar into account either. The program is operating everything in U.S. dollars.
In the cost analysis, the price assumptions stated in section 3.12 is applied. Futher-
more it is assumed, that the ship price of the Torm Lilly from the shipyard is $ 80,000,000.
A reference for shipprices can be seen in [1, p. 12 -15]. Since Torm Lilly is a chemical
tanker, the cost of purchasing it is somewhat higher than bulk carriers of the same size.
The ship is assumed written linearly oﬀover a 30 year period.
The route analysed in this section, is the Philadelphia - Gothenburg route with Torm
Lilly. A graphical presentattion of the costs, when the ship is ﬁtted with the Wärtsilä or
86
==PAGE:90==
MAN main engine, can be seen in ﬁgure 4.76 to 4.79.
Figure 4.76: Philadelphia - Gothenburg
Costs with MAN Main Engine.
Figure 4.77: Gothenburg - Philadelphia
Costs with MAN Main Engine.
Figure 4.78: Philadelphia - Gothenburg
Costs with Wärtsilä Main Engine.
Figure 4.79: Gothenburg - Philadelphia
Costs with Wärtsilä Main Engine.
In ﬁgures 4.76 to 4.79, the main engine fuel costs and writeoﬀcosts are depicted to
the vertical axis to the left in the ﬁgure. All the others costs are depicted to the vertical
axis to the right. From the ﬁgures it is shown, that the biggest costs during the operation
is the main engine fuel costs, and then the write oﬀcosts.
An easier way to depict this is seen in ﬁgure 4.80 to 4.81. These ﬁgures are more easy
to get and idea of. The ﬁgures show the total amount of dollars spent during the voyage
in the speciﬁed route. Many of the contributions calculated, are very small, why data
labels has been removed. Two colors from the ﬁgures are important, namely the blue and
orange color. The blue is the Heavy Fuel Oil costs and the orange one is the write oﬀ
costs. The ﬁgures clearly shows, that the main engine fuel costs have the largest share of
the total costs for the route.
As expected, the MAN engine are again more costly to operate than the Wärtsilä
counterpart.
One important thing to note, is that the Wärtsila maintenance costs are 10% higher
than the MAN maintenance costs. This is an assumption applied after a long and thor-
ough discussion with Man Diesel & Turbo Denmark.
4.7
Alternative Engine Analysis
As seen in the previous chapters, the Wärtsilä engine ﬁts quite nice, which were also to
be expected. On the other hand, the MAN engine did not really ﬁt to this project. This
is primarily due to the fact, that the engine is not being fully utilized. Instead of just
accepting this and move on, an attempt will be made to ﬁnd a more fair view of the MAN
engine, as the data depicted above is not fair.
First of all it could be of great interest to see, if the propeller set for this analysis
really is the optimum propeller. In order to investigate this, the program where run again
in order to study the development of the optimum pitch. One must though remember,
87
==PAGE:91==
Figure 4.80: Philadelphia - Gothenburg
Costs with MAN Main Engine.
Figure 4.81: Gothenburg - Philadelphia
Costs with MAN Main Engine.
Figure 4.82: Philadelphia - Gothenburg
Costs with Wärtsilä Main Engine.
Figure 4.83: Gothenburg - Philadelphia
Costs with Wärtsilä Main Engine.
that the optimal pitch is found with respect to the most energy eﬃcient design. This can
be seen in ﬁgure 4.84 for the Philadelphia - Gothenburg route. As it is seen, the optimum
pitch lies around 0.58, which is far from the stated 0.7, as the authors has set for this
analysis.
When doing a re-run of the simulation with the pitch found, the result is not that
good. The engine diagram is still extremely poor. Even when trying to give it a maximum
pitch, where the theory is still valid, yeilds poor results. The maximum pitch in this case
is 1.4 and performance is only hightened around 10 %. With these ﬁgures in mind, the
only real option to do is to consider to change the engine. But why is this engine used in
the ﬁrst place?
The MAN 7G50ME-C9.5-TII was selected primarily due to two concerns. The ﬁrst
concern were, that the engine had to have enough power in 100 % load to compete with
the Wärtsilä. The other concern were, that the maintenance costs over a lifetime had
to be calculated relatively precise. Man Diesel & Turbo only had data for the xG50ME
series. During the meeting with MAN Diesel & Turbo it was established, that mainte-
nance costs were no near linear between diﬀerent technical diﬀerences, e.g. change in
bore, stroke etc. To take the maintenance costs for the supplied engine family and apply
it on another would not be so beneﬁcial from their point of view.
Despite this opinion, it is the authors decision, along with good feedback from the
88
==PAGE:92==
supervisor of this thesis, to do it anyway. The maintenance costs will probably be esti-
mated somewhat to high for the MAN engine versus its Wärtsilä counterpart, but that is
an acceptable deviation. With these thoughts in mind, a new engine can be selected.
Figure 4.84: Optimum pitch for the MAN 7G50ME-C9.5-TII

and range, wherein they have their operability, can be seen in ﬁgure 4.85.
Figure 4.85: Overview of Man Diesel & Turbos engine programme.

be carried out.

4.8
Conclusion of the Analysis of Torm Lilly
It should be noticed, that all of the above is purely theoretical. The simulation also has
some assumptions and constraints, as described earlier.
As shown in the previous chapters, a full analysis were done the the Torm Lilly on
the route Philadelphia - Gothenburg and back. The analysis demonstrated the numerous
possibilities of the program, including its strength and weaknesses. An complete analysis
of the diﬀerent resistance contributions were carried out. From all of these data, the
propeller curves were calculated, along with the needed power to operate the ship for the
given ship speed. A fuel consumption analysis were carried out, which lead to a direct
cost calculation of each trip. The major contributors to the daily operational costs were
taken into account. Several minor, but still important costs, such as harbour fees, towing
fees, pilot fees etc. were left out. Concurring the fuel consumption calculations, the EEDI
numbers for emissions were also calculated. These numbers could have been calculated
with applying a scrubber to the ship, but since it is a matter of a simple math calculation
to know the emissions with and without scrubber, the authors did not do it. As Torm
Lilly is not ﬁtted with it either, this makes good sense.
Even though the program behaves as expected and gives back very meaningfull results
and numbers, the following comments is of great interest in order to give an critical, but
93
==PAGE:97==
objective assesment of the analysis.
One of the major problems in the analysis were the fuel consumption diﬀerences be-
tween the two main engine manufacturers. The BSFC curves were nothing like alike,
which gives good room for comparison. The MAN engines has a much narrower optimum
operational range, than the Wärtsilä, which seems, when looking at the numbers, as a
much more all around engine. Looking in depth on the exact power requirements for each
of the subdivided sailing ﬁelds for each of the routes, it is evident, that this is not in favor
of neither of the two MAN main engines, as the power requirements are too low. Both of
the MAN engines are only really competitive from 8,000 kW and up to their operational
limit. Much of the sailing though takes place below this limit and this brings a much
higher fuel consumption for the MAN main engines.
As discussed above and earlier, this could lead the reader to think, that the MAN
engines are of a lessor technical quality. This is most likely not the case. The more ac-
ceptable cause is, that the given propeller by the authors is not appropriate for the engine.
Perhaps even the engine choise is not appropriate one. The authors are well aware of the
fact, that the main engine and propeller comes as a propulsive package, were one must
iterate between the main engine and the propeller numerous times in order to ﬁnd the
best combination. This has not been the case for this example and that also underpinnes
the assumption, that the MAN is of no lessor technical quality then the Wärtsilä.
When looking at the total cost picture, even though the Wärtsilä is set to have 10%
higher maintenance cost, the two types of engines never levels out, as the fuel consump-
tion of the two MAN main engines are all to high. One might say, that the engines will
never run with that low of a power output, but that is not true. Multiple ships are slow
steaming today and according to MAN Diesel & Turbo, this does create additional main-
tenance for the engines. On the other hand, if the ship is intended to slow steam, they
recommend a retro ﬁt job to be done in order to get a more favorable BSFC curve.
If one of the two MAN main engines were to be used, the authors would suggest a
controllable pitch propeller, in order to keep the main engine to operate in its optimum
area. If one were to take it all to the limit, the best thing to do would probably be to
make the ship fully diesel - electric, so the MAN engine constantly operated in its best
area.
Another curiosity of the analysis is the calculated fuel consumption on the Yokohama
- San Francisco, subsequently the costs tied directly to the consumption. When compar-
ing the consumption from San Franciso - Yokohama to some of the consumption from
the other routes of almost same sailing distance, the numbers seem unreasonably low.
After having checked, checked and re-checked the calculations in the programme, a closer
scrutiny were necessary. This included investigating all of the numbers right down at the
bottom of the program and revealed a surprising discovery. By some odd coincidence,
due wind and current systems, the ship has it much more easy going from Yokohama
to San Francisco then the other way around, when looking at it from a resistance point
of view. So even though the numbers seems hard to believe, the math behind makes sense.
As expected the storm has a large eﬀect on the ship resistance in the applied ﬁeld.
The user of the program can freely choose a given area with a given wind speed. In this
simulation the chemical tanker Torm Lilly has been used with a forward speed of 12.0
knots. A storm has been applied in area 16 with a wind speed of 25 m/s. The resistances
whom gets eﬀected by the change of the wind speed is of course the wind resistance,
and added resistance due to waves, the calm water resistance does not get eﬀected by
94
==PAGE:98==
the wind speed.
The added resistances due to waves are the most eﬀected resistance
compared with the no storm case, a increase of 2,800-3,600 % can be seen. The wind
resistance is also increased, but due to the assumptions of head sea, the wind resistance
is not large compared with added resistance due to waves. The biggest wind resistance
is around 40 kN in storm and around 9 kN without storm, so the percentage diﬀerence
is still high. Another important subject in the storm is the forces due to slamming and
green water. These phenomenons are important in a real case of storm. The programme
does not take this into account in the storm simulator, so the prediction of the resistance
is only due to the wind, calm water and added wave resistance.
The economical costs for each trip does not include several types of costs. They do
not take harbour fees, diﬀerences in fuel prices across the world, tug fees, maneuvering
costs etc. The costs are relatively crude and can easily be adjusted to a more precise
number. Furthermore it is assumed that the ship sails the entered speed all the way to
the dock, which is not correct. It is often during these harbour maneuvres that the main
engine runs on very low part load with an higher fuel consumption as the result.
95
==PAGE:99==
5
30 Year Cost Analysis
As the title of this thesis states, "Total Cost of Ownership", an analysis of the ownership
costs needs to be developed. As seen previously, it could be of great interest to look
deeper into the economic diﬀerence between Torm Lilly ﬁtted with a MAN main engine
or with a Wärtsilä main engine. Unfortunately, as described earlier, the fuel consumption
for both of the found MAN engines are signiﬁcantly larger and a comparative analysis is
therefore not relevant, as the MAN ﬁtted Torm Lilly will be much more expensive over a
30 year period contra its Wärtsilä counterpart.
Instead another 30 year economical analysis can be performed. Namely an analysis
on Torm Lilly ﬁtted the Wärtsilä main engine. From the simulation chapter, several eco-
nomic parameters has been found for the routes investigated.
In order to make this analysis, with some reasonable accuracy, some additional as-
sumptions has to be made. As this thesis is not intented as an purely economical study
of trends and price development, some of the following assumptions can seem crude, but
in the overall picture they are acceptable.
• Torm Lilly is assumed to have an operational proﬁle of 80%. This means, that ship
only have sailing days / running the main engine 80% a year.
• Torm Lilly is assumed to operate all 4 routes equally frequent during its lifetime.
This means each rute is used 2,737 days.
• Maintenance work in an yard etc. is included in the maintenance costs calculated
during its voyage.
• The time, Torm Lilly is not sailing, due to yard work is included in the 80% oper-
ability assumption.
• The yearly cost increase in heavy fuel oil and marine diesel oil is set to 2.5% a year.
Technical progress in fuelsavings are assumed to be neutralized by new legislation
and more expensive equipment. A general increase in overall costs is also set to
2.5%.
• The discount factor for money is set to 10%.
• The average speed of the ship on every voyage is set to 8.0 knots.
Then slow
steaming and bad weather along with harbour maneuvring should have been taken
into account.
• The propeller, ﬁxed from the authors previously, is used as the basis again.
With all of these assumptions in place, a minor economical analysis can be carried
out. First of all, lets look at the costs associated with the diﬀerent routes. These can be
seen in table 5.1.
Table 5.1 serves as a general overview of the costs associated for Torm Lilly. With the
costs and the time for each voyage known, a yearly cost model can be made. As assumed
previously, the ship will not be sailing all of the time. This time slot amounts to a total
of 73 days each year. The 73 days not sailing amounts to $US 672,320, where as the rest
of the year amounts to $US 6,090,784, totalling yearly costs of operating Torm Lilly to
$US 6,763,104. One must though remember, that in these cost are also the write oﬀcosts,
which have a considerable size. A graphical representation of the cost distribution can be
seen in ﬁgure 5.1. As seen, the two major contributors to the yearly costs, are the main
engine fuel consumption and the write oﬀcosts.

With these ﬁgures in mind one key value can be found. The summation of the running
costs yields $US 121,275,488.35, which represents a NPV of $US 58,910,148.35. Adding
the NPV of OPEX to the CAPEX gives $US 138,910,148.35.
Over a 30 year lifetime these are the expected costs according to the authors calcu-
lations in NPV. After knowing these numbers, the feasibility of the investment can be
evaluated. The diﬀerent freight prices for chemical tankers can be analyzed and a budget
can be created.
An important note to the calculations above is, that the interests from the company
or investers has not been set. The interest rates can diﬀer widely and they are of greater
interest when looking at the cash ﬂow and revenues generated for the ship. The authors
has been unable to obtain meaningfull insight into the economical situation sourrounding
the Torm Lilly. Due to this, the 30 year analysis has only been looking at the expenditures
part of the case.
Also due to the missing insight into the ship economics, a dedicated economical analy-
sis module, has not been developed. All the numbers, to support such a module, is though
readily available and only the programming of the diﬀerent economical schemes needs to
be done. This has been done purposefully, as an economical analysis module should be a
part of the future development of the programme.
98
==PAGE:102==
==PAGE:103==
6
Discussion
During the progress of this thesis, several ideas and concerns arose, which needs a deeper
explanation. These ideas and concerns are being dealt with in this chapter. Many of the
ideas and thoughts discussed in this chapter can serve as inspiration for further develop-
ment or reﬁnement of the programme.
One of the most important areas of the analysis is the analysis of the diﬀerent types
of resistances. In this case, the program is applying the wind resistance, calm water resis-
tance and added resistance due to waves. But these kind of resistances are not the only
one surrounding the ship.Very early in the thesis the assumption were made, that the
program would only look into sailing conditions with zero trim. This is a minor inaccu-
racy, as allmost all ships sailing on the oceans have some sort of trim during their voyage.
When a ship sails with trim, a special lift case arises. Due to this fact, it is actually
favorable for all ships to sail with some kind of trim in order to minimize the resistance
through the water. As mentioned earlier, this inﬂuence of trim is hard to incorporate and
due to simpliﬁcation reasons, this particular area has been left out.
When doing the voyage distance calculations in the STM, a set of waypoint coordi-
nates, which can make the route more or less detailed, with bigger or smaller distances
in between each waypoint, are being utilized for the calculation of the sailing distance.
When doing this distance calculation, the ship is assumed to sail in a direct line. This
is also an assumption which have been made by simplicity reasons. Future development
into this module would be the incorporation of driftforces from the WRM, CRWM and
ARWM, so the route gets a more realistic look. The contributions from all of the direc-
tions of the diﬀerent components to ship are already in place for future development.
At the same time, the contributions from following seas could be incorporated. Follow-
ing seas can have a signiﬁcant impact on the overall resistance during a voyage, especially
on the eastern bound routes over e.g. the North Atlantic.
The limitations within the STM also shows, when the user enters a speed into the
programme. Here two options are possible, either the ﬁxed time or ﬁxed speed during the
chosen voyage. Behind these two choices is the same restriction, namely that the program
assumes a ﬁxed speed over ground during the entire voyage. This is also an assumption
made partly due to simplicity, but partly due to restraints in the overall construction of
the programme. The idea of having variable speeds across the voyage came very late in
the programming part and the programme were already to locked to incorporate that
feature. A single run, if this feature were incorporated, would prolong the calculation
time of the program more than 90 times. For future development it would be clearly
beneﬁcial to change some of the underlying structures, so diﬀerent speeds are possible.
This would also mean, that when the ship meets some rough and harsh areas, the needed
power in the given ﬁeld will not be bigger then the power installed in the ship. This
would also make all the numbers calculated after the EAM more accurate. When doing
this change, a check for slamming and green water on deck could also be carried out, as
excessive speed in given seastates could result in these phenomenons. The program is
already prepared for this extension, as the average speed of the crossing is already being
calculated and be seen in the MWAM
When applying the expansion of the STM just mentioned, a more realistic analysis of
a storm will be made. As seen previously, the resistance is very big in the storm with 25
m/s wind and sailing 12.0 knots. As the authors are well aware of, it is not realistic to
keep a speed over ground of 12 knots in those weather conditions. If the program could
check against the engine power installed, then the real speed over ground and through
100
==PAGE:104==
water could be iterated, in order to assess if the maneuvrebility speed is suﬃcient to sail
in that kind of weather. Furthermore it could serve as a great tool to see in what weather
conditions the ship should change route or stay at port.
The pre-embedded dual coordinate system in the WRM, CRWM and ARWM is some-
what limiting the freedom of the ships motions. One the one hand, a smaller gap between
each waypoint can be selected to make a route more realistic, but it still limites the accu-
racy of the resistance calculations. Waves and currents comes from all kinds of directions
and it would therefore beneﬁcial to build in more angles, where the resistance can come
from. This would give a better and more accurate description of the resistances encoun-
tered during a voyage.
During the design of the programme, a more technical approach to the programme
were undertaken. Nervertheless has all supportive functions for new developments of cost
modules been prepared in the underlying stream of data. The prices used for the analy-
sis are only the major contributors, as all minor expenditures such as harbour fees, tug
fees etc. has not been taken into account. This is mainly due to the lack of data on
these areas, but also due to the more technical approach to the programme. The accu-
racy of the cost data obtained are regarded as suﬃciently high, to make conclusions upon.
The theory, creating the fundation for the programme, is found after having applied
the assumptions for the programme. The limitations of these theories has been respected
all through the programming and the programmes limitations has been described earlier
upon. This could be the assumptions regarding the SMB method, so a user do not cre-
ate a storm in Suez Channel. Another example could be the use of the Salvesen theory.
Another choice could be to use theory created Faltinsen, but this theory were limited to
smaller frequency span, only encountered up to 50 km from shore. For simplicity, this
were left out, as the contribution from Faltinsen to the overall resistance would be neg-
ligable. The wave spectrum, applied in the programme, also holds the prerequiste that
it is a fully developed sea at deep water. A good way to expand the programme would
be to incorporate future theory, which will increase the validity area of the programme
in thereby making it overall more applicable to diﬀerent situations encountered by the
shipowners.
Many of the extensions mentioned above, could be incorporated into new modules.
Other modules, for future development, could be trading modules, freight modules, costs
related to time in port modules etc. Several new technical modules, such as strip theory,
automatic load of new ships or routes, could also be built in, in order to make the program
a better analysis tool.
With respect to the future use of the program, to paths can be selected. The ﬁrst path
is to use the program as an analysis tool at the oﬃce, where evaluation of the diﬀerent
ships can be done.
The other path is to attach the programme to the measurement
programme, which are onboard on more and more ships, e.g. the Torm Lilly. By doing
so, all of the empirical data, precoded into the program, will be exchanged with a series
of live measurements (heave, pitch, fuel consumption etc.). The reason for doing so, is
that the program now can be used as an operational guidance tool, where a time step
of x hours can be chosen. After having sailed the x number of hours, the real costs of
operation the ship is known and these costs can be compared with the costs found by the
program. All of this in order to optimize the ship enroute, to ensure the most beneﬁcial
and optimal cost operation24.
24The authors are disregarding the human factors in this and are only looking at the purely technical
side. Human factors would call for several other considerations.
101
==PAGE:105==
7
Conclusion
The purpose of this master thesis was to develop an analytical tool for analysing the Total
Costs of Ownership. The programme is targeting both the shipowners and operaters, but
also the future maritime students at DTU. During the progress of the master thesis, due
some unforeseen events, the thesis took a more technical path, although cost analysis is
still a part of it.
The program developed is able to predict and calculate the wind resistance, calm
water resistance and added resistiance due to waves for several diﬀerent ships on numerous
routes for all forward speeds. When having found these parameters, the program is able
to ﬁnd the optimum propeller pitch and thereby yhe necessary engine power needed.
Furthermore the program is able to calculate the fuel consumption of both main and
auxiliary machinery, the EEDI emissions from these, along with all of the costs associated
with a voyage.
In addition to this, the program has been extended to incorporate severeal features,
such as the implementation of storms on a ships voyage, the application of scrubbers and
the ability to assign an own designed propeller to the ship.
The programme is thereby able to test and compare several diﬀerent ships with charac-
teristics on the same route in order to ﬁnd the most optimum solution, both with respect
to technical considerations, but also with respect to economical considerations.
The most typical ships and routes has been precoded into the program and due to
the modular construction of the program, new ships and routes can be easily implemented.
During the testing and validation of the program an analysis of the ship Torm Lilly
is done. The analysis of Torm Lilly showed that the engine, actually used in Torm Lilly,
is well choosen. An comparison were made to two MAN engines, of which the Wärtsilä
were the most economical.
During the 30 year analysis subsequently done with Torm Lilly, it were found, that the
lifetime operational costs of the Torm Lilly almost is at the same order as the newbuilding
costs.
As a last remark, the program has been coded and built in an open modular way,
which makes future implementation of additional modules and tweaks of the program
faster and easier.
102

Appendix
9.1
Selv evaluering
Denne selvevaluering er skrevet samlet for både Isa og Niels.
Projektet lagde ud med et tæt samarbejde med Man Diesel & Turbo, da projektet
kom som et oplæg fra selvsamme. Pga. forskellige omstændigheder blev mødet først sat
i værk d. 19 juni 2014, hvilket var meget langt fremme i processen.
Pga. dette blev det efter aftale med vores fælles vejleder, Poul Andersen, sat i værk,
at programmet og hele masteren skulle have en mindre økonomisk tyngde og en mere
teknisk tyngde. Dette muliggjorde bl.a. at forskellige funktioner til frit valg af propeller
dimensioner og valg af storm påudvalgte dele af rejsen blev muliggjort.
Når man sammenholder den oprindelige projektplan, lavet indenfor den første måned
af projektet, med den endelige projektplan, såer der en stor forskel at spore. Både mht.
til omfanget af de enkelte delopgaver, men ogsåmængden af opgaver er væsentligt større,
da undertegnede har modtaget stor inspiration fra vores vejleder. Uagtet af dette, mener
vi stadig, at programmet og masteren holder et godt akademisk niveau og ikke falder
igennem pga. de øgede mængder af opgaver. Dette ses især i selve kodedelen.
Undervejs har det været et gavnligt og bæredygtigt arbejde at være to omkring denne
master. Det har givet os begge to øgede styrker og erfaring indenfor programmering,
overordnet sammensætning af mange maritime områder, samt at skulle lave et projekt-
samarbejde påtværs af ﬂere teknologiske platforme. Et eksempel herpåer, at Isa både var
optaget af et andet 3 ugers fag i perioden, jeg var ude at rejse lidt, samt at ramadanen
kom i slutningen af projektet. Dette betød bl.a. at vi ﬂyttede arbejdsbyrden i døgnet og
primørt arbejdede aften, nat og morgen.
Alt i alt er vi begge glade for det stærke faglige udbytte vi har fået ud af projektet,
samt de øgede samarbejdsevner, som var essentielle, for at komme godt i mål.
Isa Ilkan Duran (s083190)
Niels Hvid (s082875)

calculated propeller

      subroutine ktkqx(Aj,x,y,z,Diam,n5,tcs,tc,rnx,k1,k3,k1noz)
c
c**********************************************************************
c
c   This subroutine calculates Kt and Kq for given J for various
c   propellers:
c
c      Wageningen standard B- and BB-series
c      Wageningen nozzle propellers
c      Extrapolations based on Wageningen standard series
c         and unser-defined characteristics
c
c                                                   23. March 1994
c*********************************************************************
c
c
  100 continue
      if (inoz.eq.1) goto 110
      if (inoz.eq.2) goto 130
      if (inoz.eq.3) goto 140
      if (inoz.eq.4) goto 150
      if (inoz.eq.5) goto 160
      if (inoz.eq.6) goto 170
      if (inoz.eq.7) goto 120
      if (inoz.eq.8) goto 180
c
c   Wageningen standard B-series
c
      K1=.00880496-.204554*Aj+.166351*X+.158114*X**2-.147581*Aj**2*Y
     *   -.481497*Aj*X*Y+.415437*X**2*Y+.0144043*Z-.0530054*Aj**2*Z
     *   +.0143481*X*Z+.0606826*Aj*X*Z-.0125894*Y*Z
      K1=K1+.0109689*Aj*Y*Z-.133698*X**3+.00638407*X**6
     *   -.00132718*Aj**2*X**6+.168496*Aj**3*Y-.0507214*Y**2
     *   +.0854559*Aj**2*Y**2-.0504475*Aj**3*Y**2+.010465*Aj*X**6*Y**2
      K1=K1-.00648272*Aj**2*X**6*Y**2-.00841728*X**3*Z
     *   +.0168424*Aj*X**3*Z-.00102296*Aj**3*X**3*Z-.0317791*X**3*Y*Z
     *   +.018604*Aj*Y**2*Z-.00410798*X**2*Y**2*Z
      K1=K1-.000606848*Z**2-.0049819*Aj*Z**2+.0025983*Aj**2*Z**2
     *   -.000560528*Aj**3*Z**2-.00163652*Aj*X**2*Z**2
     *   -.000328787*Aj*X**6*Z**2+.000116502*Aj**2*X**6*Z**2
     *   +.000690904*Y*Z**2
      K1=K1+.00421749*X**3*Y*Z**2+.0000565229*Aj**3*X**6*Y*Z**2
     *   -.00146564*X**3*Y**2*Z**2
      IF (Ius.eq.1) goto 102
      Rn0=Rnx
      IF (Rnx.gt.0.01d0) goto 101
      Rn0=2.22*Diam*Y*SQRT(Aj*Aj+(.75*PI())**2)*N5*Diam/Z/Visco*1000000
  101 Rn0=DEXP(4.6052+SQRT((1.+2.*Tcs)/(1.+2.*Tc))*(DLOG(Rn0)-4.6052))
      IF (Rn0.le.2.d6) Rn0=2.d6
      IF (Rn0.ge.2.d10) Rn0=2.d10
      Lrn=DLOG10(Rn0)-.301
  102 K1=K1+.000353485-.00333758*Y*Aj*Aj-.00478125*Y*X*Aj
     *   +.000257792*Lrn**2*Y*Aj*Aj+.0000643192*Lrn*X**6*Aj*Aj
     *   -.0000110636*Lrn**2*X**6*Aj*Aj-.0000276305*Lrn**2*Z*Y*Aj*Aj
      K1=K1+.0000954*Lrn*Z*Y*X*Aj+.0000032049*Lrn*Z*Z*Y*X**3*Aj
c
      K3=.00379368+.00886523*Aj**2-.032241*Aj*X+.00344778*X**2
     *   -.0408811*X*Y-.108009*Aj*X*Y-.0885381*Aj**2*X*Y+.188561*X**2*Y
     *   -.00370871*Aj*Z+.00513696*X*Z+.0209449*Aj*X*Z
      K3=K3+.00474319*Aj**2*X*Z-.00723408*Aj**2*Y*Z+.00438388*Aj*X*Y*Z

     *   -.0269403*X**2*Y*Z+.0558082*Aj**3*Y+.0161886*X**3*Y

      K3=K3+.0196283*Aj**3*Y**2-.0502782*X*Y**2-.030055*Aj**3*X*Y**2
     *   +.0417122*Aj**2*X**2*Y**2-.0397722*X**3*Y**2
      K3=K3-.00350024*X**6*Y**2-.0106854*Aj**3*Z+.00110903*Aj**3*X**3*Z
      K3=K3-.000313912*X**6*Z+.0035985*Aj**3*Y*Z-.00142121*X**6*Y*Z

      K3=K3-.00183491*X*Z**2*Aj

      K3=K3-.000591412+.00696898*X-.0000666654*Z*X**6+.0160818*Y*Y

      K3=K3-.00000088528*Lrn**2*Z*Y*X*Aj+.0000230171*Lrn*Z*X**6

      GOTO 200

A) Sound pressure levels from exhaust gas system (2x10-5 Pa).
The expected sound pressure level at 1 metre from the edge of the exhaust gas pipe opening at an angle of 30 degrees to the direction 
of the gas flow and valid for a normal exhaust gas system - but without a boiler and silencer.
B) Airborne sound pressure levels - with standard noise reduction (NR) countermeasures (2x10-5 Pa).
Expected mean sound pressure octave spectrum levels, i.e. the average spatial noise values at a distance of 1 metre from the engine.
Prescribed measuring surface area is 558.1 m².
C) Air-borne sound pressure levels - with additional noise reduction (NR) countermeasures (2x10-5 Pa).
Expected mean sound pressure octave spectrum levels, i.e. the average spatial noise values at a distance of 1 metre from the engine.
Prescribed measuring surface area is 558.1 m².
Additional noise reduction countermeasures, e.g.:
Extra good turbocharger air intake silencer(s)
External sound insulation of scavenge air receiver
External sound insulation of scavenge air cooler(s).
Supplementary reduction of 0.0 dB is needed.
Other additional noise reduction countermeasures are also available. The noise figures given are in accordance with the CIMAC 
recommendations for measurements of the overall noise for reciprocating engines. The average levels will, depending on the actual 
engine room configuration, be 1-5 dB higher when the engine is installed in the engine room.
D) Structure borne vibration levels (5x10-8 Pa).
Expected mean velocity octave spectrum levels at the engine base plate as installed on board the ship. Based on an average engine 
foundation of a ship, and may only be used as a rough estimate as the velocity levels will depend on the actual foundation used. If the 
vibration velocity levels are referred to 10-9 m/s instead of 5x10-8 m/s, the calculated dB figures will be 34.0 dB higher than above stated.

A) Sound pressure levels from exhaust gas system (2x10-5 Pa).
The expected sound pressure level at 1 metre from the edge of the exhaust gas pipe opening at an angle of 30 degrees to the direction 
of the gas flow and valid for a normal exhaust gas system - but without a boiler and silencer.
B) Airborne sound pressure levels - with standard noise reduction (NR) countermeasures (2x10-5 Pa).
Expected mean sound pressure octave spectrum levels, i.e. the average spatial noise values at a distance of 1 metre from the engine.
Prescribed measuring surface area is 484.0 m².
C) Air-borne sound pressure levels - with additional noise reduction (NR) countermeasures (2x10-5 Pa).
Expected mean sound pressure octave spectrum levels, i.e. the average spatial noise values at a distance of 1 metre from the engine.
Prescribed measuring surface area is 484.0 m².
Additional noise reduction countermeasures, e.g.:
Extra good turbocharger air intake silencer(s)
External sound insulation of scavenge air receiver
External sound insulation of scavenge air cooler(s).
Supplementary reduction of 0.0 dB is needed.
Other additional noise reduction countermeasures are also available. The noise figures given are in accordance with the CIMAC 
recommendations for measurements of the overall noise for reciprocating engines. The average levels will, depending on the actual 
engine room configuration, be 1-5 dB higher when the engine is installed in the engine room.
D) Structure borne vibration levels (5x10-8 Pa).
Expected mean velocity octave spectrum levels at the engine base plate as installed on board the ship. Based on an average engine 
foundation of a ship, and may only be used as a rough estimate as the velocity levels will depend on the actual foundation used. If the 
vibration velocity levels are referred to 10-9 m/s instead of 5x10-8 m/s, the calculated dB figures will be 34.0 dB higher than above stated.

A) Sound pressure levels from exhaust gas system (2x10-5 Pa).
The expected sound pressure level at 1 metre from the edge of the exhaust gas pipe opening at an angle of 30 degrees to the direction 
of the gas flow and valid for a normal exhaust gas system - but without a boiler and silencer.
B) Airborne sound pressure levels - with standard noise reduction (NR) countermeasures (2x10-5 Pa).
Expected mean sound pressure octave spectrum levels, i.e. the average spatial noise values at a distance of 1 metre from the engine.
Prescribed measuring surface area is 321.2 m².
C) Air-borne sound pressure levels - with additional noise reduction (NR) countermeasures (2x10-5 Pa).
Expected mean sound pressure octave spectrum levels, i.e. the average spatial noise values at a distance of 1 metre from the engine.
Prescribed measuring surface area is 321.2 m².
Additional noise reduction countermeasures, e.g.:
Extra good turbocharger air intake silencer(s)
External sound insulation of scavenge air receiver
External sound insulation of scavenge air cooler(s).
Supplementary reduction of 0.0 dB is needed.
Other additional noise reduction countermeasures are also available. The noise figures given are in accordance with the CIMAC 
recommendations for measurements of the overall noise for reciprocating engines. The average levels will, depending on the actual 
engine room configuration, be 1-5 dB higher when the engine is installed in the engine room.
D) Structure borne vibration levels (5x10-8 Pa).
Expected mean velocity octave spectrum levels at the engine base plate as installed on board the ship. Based on an average engine 
foundation of a ship, and may only be used as a rough estimate as the velocity levels will depend on the actual foundation used. If the 
vibration velocity levels are referred to 10-9 m/s instead of 5x10-8 m/s, the calculated dB figures will be 34.0 dB higher than above stated.

A) Sound pressure levels from exhaust gas system (2x10-5 Pa).
The expected sound pressure level at 1 metre from the edge of the exhaust gas pipe opening at an angle of 30 degrees to the direction 
of the gas flow and valid for a normal exhaust gas system - but without a boiler and silencer.
B) Airborne sound pressure levels - with standard noise reduction (NR) countermeasures (2x10-5 Pa).
Expected mean sound pressure octave spectrum levels, i.e. the average spatial noise values at a distance of 1 metre from the engine.
Prescribed measuring surface area is 321.2 m².
C) Air-borne sound pressure levels - with additional noise reduction (NR) countermeasures (2x10-5 Pa).
Expected mean sound pressure octave spectrum levels, i.e. the average spatial noise values at a distance of 1 metre from the engine.
Prescribed measuring surface area is 321.2 m².
Additional noise reduction countermeasures, e.g.:
Extra good turbocharger air intake silencer(s)
External sound insulation of scavenge air receiver
External sound insulation of scavenge air cooler(s).
Supplementary reduction of 0.0 dB is needed.
Other additional noise reduction countermeasures are also available. The noise figures given are in accordance with the CIMAC 
recommendations for measurements of the overall noise for reciprocating engines. The average levels will, depending on the actual 
engine room configuration, be 1-5 dB higher when the engine is installed in the engine room.
D) Structure borne vibration levels (5x10-8 Pa).
Expected mean velocity octave spectrum levels at the engine base plate as installed on board the ship. Based on an average engine 
foundation of a ship, and may only be used as a rough estimate as the velocity levels will depend on the actual foundation used. If the 
vibration velocity levels are referred to 10-9 m/s instead of 5x10-8 m/s, the calculated dB figures will be 34.0 dB higher than above stated.

function varargout = GUI_USER(varargin)
% GUI_USER MATLAB code for GUI_USER.fig
%      GUI_USER, by itself, creates a new GUI_USER or raises the existing
%      singleton*.
%
%      H = GUI_USER returns the handle to a new GUI_USER or the handle to
%      the existing singleton*.
%
%      GUI_USER('CALLBACK',hObject,eventData,handles,...) calls the local
%      function named CALLBACK in GUI_USER.M
%      with the given input arguments.
%
%    GUI_USER('Property','Value',...) creates a new GUI_USER or raises the
%   existing singleton*.  Starting from the left, property value pairs are
%     applied to the GUI before GUI_USER_OpeningFcn gets called.  An
%    unrecognized property name or invalid value makes property application
%      stop.  All inputs are passed to GUI_USER_OpeningFcn via varargin.
%
%      *See GUI Options on GUIDE's Tools menu.  Choose "GUI allows only one
%      instance to run (singleton)".
%
% See also: GUIDE, GUIDATA, GUIHANDLES
% Edit the above text to modify the response to help GUI_USER
% Last Modified by GUIDE v2.5 31-Jul-2014 13:13:42
% Begin initialization code - DO NOT EDIT
gui_Singleton = 1;
gui_State = struct('gui_Name',       mfilename, ...
                   'gui_Singleton',  gui_Singleton, ...
                   'gui_OpeningFcn', @GUI_USER_OpeningFcn, ...
                   'gui_OutputFcn',  @GUI_USER_OutputFcn, ...
                   'gui_LayoutFcn',  [] , ...
                   'gui_Callback',   []);
if nargin && ischar(varargin{1})
    gui_State.gui_Callback = str2func(varargin{1});
end
if nargout
    [varargout{1:nargout}] = gui_mainfcn(gui_State, varargin{:});
else
    gui_mainfcn(gui_State, varargin{:});
end
% End initialization code - DO NOT EDIT

function varargout = GUI(varargin)
% GUI MATLAB code for GUI.fig
%      GUI, by itself, creates a new GUI or raises the existing
%      singleton*.
%
%      H = GUI returns the handle to a new GUI or the handle to
%      the existing singleton*.
%
%      GUI('CALLBACK',hObject,eventData,handles,...) calls the local
%      function named CALLBACK in GUI.M with the given input arguments.
%
%      GUI('Property','Value',...) creates a new GUI or raises the
%      existing singleton*.  Starting from the left, property value pairs
 %     are
%      applied to the GUI before GUI_OpeningFcn gets called.  An
%      unrecognized property name or invalid value makes property
%      application
%      stop.  All inputs are passed to GUI_OpeningFcn via varargin.
%
%      *See GUI Options on GUIDE's Tools menu.  Choose "GUI allows only one
%      instance to run (singleton)".
%
% See also: GUIDE, GUIDATA, GUIHANDLES
% Edit the above text to modify the response to help GUI
% Last Modified by GUIDE v2.5 01-Aug-2014 10:58:21
% Begin initialization code - DO NOT EDIT
gui_Singleton = 1;
gui_State = struct('gui_Name',       mfilename, ...
                   'gui_Singleton',  gui_Singleton, ...
                   'gui_OpeningFcn', @GUI_OpeningFcn, ...
                   'gui_OutputFcn',  @GUI_OutputFcn, ...
                   'gui_LayoutFcn',  [] , ...
                   'gui_Callback',   []);
if nargin && ischar(varargin{1})
    gui_State.gui_Callback = str2func(varargin{1});
end
if nargout
    [varargout{1:nargout}] = gui_mainfcn(gui_State, varargin{:});
else
    gui_mainfcn(gui_State, varargin{:});
end
% End initialization code - DO NOT EDIT
% --- Executes just before GUI is made visible.
function GUI_OpeningFcn(hObject, eventdata, handles, varargin)
% This function has no output args, see OutputFcn.
% hObject    handle to figure
==PAGE:255==
2
% eventdata  reserved - to be defined in a future version of MATLAB
% handles    structure with handles and user data (see GUIDATA)
% varargin   command line arguments to GUI (see VARARGIN)
guiresult = getappdata(0,'guiresult');
if guiresult == 1; %Showing the results for the route choosen
Lpp = evalin ('base','Lpp');
D = evalin ('base','D');
T = evalin ('base','T');

function varargout = GUI_total(varargin)
% GUI_TOTAL MATLAB code for GUI_total.fig
%      GUI_TOTAL, by itself, creates a new GUI_TOTAL or raises the existing
%      singleton*.
%
%      H = GUI_TOTAL returns the handle to a new GUI_TOTAL or the handle to
%      the existing singleton*.
%
%      GUI_TOTAL('CALLBACK',hObject,eventData,handles,...) calls the local
%      function named CALLBACK in GUI_TOTAL.M with the given input
%      arguments.
%
%      GUI_TOTAL('Property','Value',...) creates a new GUI_TOTAL or
%      raises the
%      existing singleton*.  Starting from the left, property value
%      pairs are
%      applied to the GUI before GUI_total_OpeningFcn gets called.  An
%      unrecognized property name or invalid value makes property
%      application
%      stop.  All inputs are passed to GUI_total_OpeningFcn via varargin.
%
%      *See GUI Options on GUIDE's Tools menu.  Choose "GUI allows only one
%      instance to run (singleton)".
%
% See also: GUIDE, GUIDATA, GUIHANDLES
% Edit the above text to modify the response to help GUI_total
% Last Modified by GUIDE v2.5 03-Aug-2014 15:33:06
% Begin initialization code - DO NOT EDIT
gui_Singleton = 1;
gui_State = struct('gui_Name',       mfilename, ...
                   'gui_Singleton',  gui_Singleton, ...
                   'gui_OpeningFcn', @GUI_total_OpeningFcn, ...
                   'gui_OutputFcn',  @GUI_total_OutputFcn, ...
                   'gui_LayoutFcn',  [] , ...
                   'gui_Callback',   []);
if nargin && ischar(varargin{1})
    gui_State.gui_Callback = str2func(varargin{1});
end
if nargout
    [varargout{1:nargout}] = gui_mainfcn(gui_State, varargin{:});
else
    gui_mainfcn(gui_State, varargin{:});
end

% --- Executes on button press in weather.
function weather_Callback(hObject, eventdata, handles)
% hObject    handle to weather (see GCBO)
% eventdata  reserved - to be defined in a future version of MATLAB
% handles    structure with handles and user data (see GUIDATA)
SR = evalin('base','SR_we');
A104 = evalin('base','A104');
figure
subplot(2,1,1)
bar(1:length(SR),A104(SR,9,1),0.2)
title('Mean wave height for each passed area','FontSize',24)
xlabel('Area','FontSize',22)
ylim([0,max(A104(SR,9,1))])
ylabel('Wave height [m]','FontSize',22)
for i=1:length(SR)
set(gca, 'XTick',1:length(SR))
set(gca,'XTickLabel',SR(1:length(SR(i)):length(SR)))
set(gca,'FontSize',16)
end
subplot(2,1,2)
bar(1:length(SR),A104(SR,9,2),0.2)
title('Mean wave periode for each passed area','FontSize',24)
xlabel('Area','FontSize',22)
ylim([0,max(A104(SR,9,2))])
ylabel('Wave periode [sec]','FontSize',22)
for i=1:length(SR)
set(gca, 'XTick',1:length(SR))
==PAGE:268==
3
set(gca,'XTickLabel',SR(1:length(SR(i)):length(SR)))
set(gca,'FontSize',16)
end

% --- Executes on button press in wavespec.
function wavespec_Callback(hObject, eventdata, handles)
% hObject    handle to wavespec (see GCBO)
% eventdata  reserved - to be defined in a future version of MATLAB
% handles    structure with handles and user data (see GUIDATA)
SR=evalin('base','SR_we');
Sp_we=evalin('base','Sp_we');
omega=evalin('base','omega');
%k={'North-West','North','North-East','West','','East','South-West',
%'South','South-East'};
for j=1:length(SR) %Number of fields & SR
 figure
 % This creates the 'background' axes
ha = axes('units','normalized', ...
            'position',[0 0 1 1]);
% Move the background axes to the bottom
uistack(ha,'bottom');
% Load in a background image and display it using the correct colors
% The image used below, is in the Image Processing Toolbox.  If you do not
% have %access to this toolbox, you can use another image file instead.
I=imread('compass.jpg');
hi = imagesc(I);
colormap gray
% Turn the handlevisibility off so that we don't inadvertently plot into
% the axes again
% Also, make the axes invisible
set(ha,'handlevisibility','off', ...
            'visible','off')
% Now we can use the figure, as required.
% For example, we can put a plot in an axes
axes('position',[0.3,0.35,0.4,0.4])

4
 for i=[1:4 6:9] %Creating row x collums for subplot and number of headings
subplot(3,3,i)
plot(omega,Sp_we(:,1:length(i),j))
xlabel('\omega [rad/s]')
ylabel('S(\omega)')
 end
suptitle(['Wave spectrum for area ', num2str(SR(j))])
% this creates transparency, you probably dont need it:
set(hi,'Alphadata',.4)
% move the image to the top:
  uistack(ha,'top');
% copyobj(get(h1,'children'),h1); %This makes it come up in a new window
end

function Propeller_Diagram_we_Callback(hObject, eventdata, handles)
% hObject    handle to Propeller_Diagram_we (see GCBO)

% eventdata  reserved - to be defined in a future version of MATLAB

%PD = evalin('base','PD');

set(hXL,'position',pXL,'horizontalalign','right')
hYL=ylabel({'K_t';'10K_Q';'\eta'},'rot',0,'FontSize',22);
pYL=get(hYL,'position'); pYL(2)=0.8;     % adjust to top axis limit
set(hYL,'position',pYL,'horizontalalign','right')
title('Characteristic of Wageningen B-series propellers','FontSize',24)
set(gca,'FontSize',22)
end

L2 = [A(1,3),(A(end,2)/A(end,3))*A(1,3)];

% --- Executes on button press in Total_cost_time_we.
function Total_cost_time_we_Callback(hObject, eventdata, handles)
% hObject    handle to Total_cost_time_we (see GCBO)
% eventdata  reserved - to be defined in a future version of MATLAB
% handles    structure with handles and user data (see GUIDATA)
Prop_we = evalin('base','Prop_we');
Joinedcost_time_we= evalin('base','Joinedcost_time_we');
time = evalin('base','time');
FPG  = evalin('base','FPG');
for i      = 1:FPG
    [x,y]   = max(Prop_we(4,:,i));
h(1,i)     =y;
ef(1,i)    =Joinedcost_time_we(i,h(1,i),1);
af(1,i)    =Joinedcost_time_we(i,h(1,i),2);
sa(1,i)    =Joinedcost_time_we(i,h(1,i),3);
misc(1,i)  =Joinedcost_time_we(i,h(1,i),4);
main(1,i)  =Joinedcost_time_we(i,h(1,i),5);
wo(1,i)    =Joinedcost_time_we(i,h(1,i),6);
t(1,i)     =Joinedcost_time_we(i,h(1,i),7);
end
sum(ef)
sum(af);
sum(sa);
sum(misc);
sum(main);
sum(wo);
tt = cumsum ( time );
zero = 0 ;
tt = [zero tt] ;
tt = tt(1:end-1) ;
H1 = figure;
[ha, h1, h2] = plotyy(tt,ef,tt,af,'stairs');
set(ha,{'ycolor'},{'k';'k'})
set(h1,{'color'},{'r'})
set(h2,{'color'},{'b'})
set(ha(1),'YLim',[0 25000])
set(ha(1),'YTick',[0:4000:25000])
set(ha(2),'YLim',[0 150])
set(ha(2),'YTick',[0:30:150])
hold(ha(2), 'on');
h3 = stairs(ha(2), tt, sa);
set(h3, {'color'},{'k'})
hold(ha(1), 'on');
h6 = stairs(ha(1), tt, wo);
==PAGE:299==
34
set(h6, {'color'},{'c'})
h4 = stairs(ha(2), tt, misc);
set(h4, {'color'},{'m'})
h5 = stairs(ha(2), tt, main);
set(h5, {'color'},{'g'})
legend('Main Engine Fuel Cost','Writeoff Cost','Aux. Engine Fuel Cost',...
    'Salary Cost','Misc Cost','Maintenance Cost')
xlabel('Total voyage time [Hours]','FontSize',22)
set(get(ha(2),'Ylabel'),'string','Cost [$]','FontSize',22)

    'Salary Cost','Misc Cost','Maintenance Cost')

% eventdata  reserved - to be defined in a future version of MATLAB
% handles    structure with handles and user data (see GUIDATA)
FPG  = evalin('base','FPG')
BSFC = ones(1,FPG)*77;
time = evalin('base','time')
BSFC = ones(1,18).*(BSFC.*time);
h1   = figure();
stairs(1:FPG,BSFC)
title('Auxiliary Engine Fuel Consumption West-East','FontSize',24)
xlabel('Fields','FontSize',22)
ylabel('BSFC [Kg]','FontSize',22)
set(gca,'FontSize',16)
copyobj(get(h1,'children'),h1); %This makes it come up in a new window

% --- Executes on button press in Aux_Fuel_time_ew.
function Aux_Fuel_time_ew_Callback(hObject, eventdata, handles)
% hObject    handle to Aux_Fuel_time_ew (see GCBO)
% eventdata  reserved - to be defined in a future version of MATLAB
% handles    structure with handles and user data (see GUIDATA)
FPG = evalin('base','FPG')
BSFC = ones(1,FPG)*77; %77kg/h
time = evalin('base','time')
time = flip(time)
tt = cumsum ( time ) ;
zero = 0 ;
tt = [zero tt] ;
tt = tt(1:end-1) ;
h1 = figure();
stairs(tt,BSFC)
title('Auxiliary Engine Fuel Consumption East-West','FontSize',24)
xlabel('Time [Hours]','FontSize',22)
ylabel('BSFC [Kg]','FontSize',22)
set(gca,'FontSize',16)
copyobj(get(h1,'children'),h1); %This makes it come up in a new window

function [Res_max_we_final,Res_min_we_final,Res_ave_weplus,...
    Res_ave_weminus,Res_max_ew_final,Res_min_ew_final,...
    Res_ave_ewplus,Res_ave_ewminus,Rw_we,Rw_ew,Rw_we_fieldsumplus,...
    Rw_ew_fieldsumplus,FPG,Rw_ew_final,Rw_we_final] =...
    Wind_Module(Lpp,B,D,T,Vsin,rhoair15,Loa,Route,ship,...
    storm,s_fieldwind,s_wind)
% Given ship dimension
% clear all
% Lpp  = 174.17;
% B    = 32.20;
% D    = 18.53;
% T    = 12.39;
%Vsin = 15; % Service speed in knots
 Vs   = Vsin*0.5144; % Ship speed [m/s]
% rhoair15  = 1.226; %kg/m^3 for 15 deg. air
% Loa = 195;
 L=Loa;
% % Properties
SR = [-1/4*pi 0*pi 1/4*pi 2/4*pi 3/4*pi 4/4*pi -3/4*pi -2/4*pi]; %Radians
% for directions in order NW, N, NE, E, SE, S, SW
Beaufort = [0.2 0.9 2.4 4.4 6.7 9.4 12.3 15.5 19.0 22.6 26.5 30.5 34.0];
% The beaufort scale in m/s
if Route == 1;
    load Phillygott.mat
    Phillygott_we = Phillygott;
    FPG = 18; %Fields in the sailing trip
    %The predefined sailing route for Philadelphia - Gothenburg the ship
    % during the fields.
    SRPG =[SR(1,3) SR(1,3) SR(1,3) SR(1,3) SR(1,4) SR(1,4) SR(1,4)...
        SR(1,4) SR(1,4) SR(1,4) SR(1,4) SR(1,4) SR(1,4)...
        SR(1,4) SR(1,3) SR(1,3) SR(1,3) SR(1,4)];
if storm == 1 % Doing the manipulation of the matrix with storm
    for i=1:length(s_fieldwind)
    Phillygott_we(s_fieldwind(1,i),:,1) = 0; %Nulling all the percentages
    Phillygott_we(s_fieldwind(1,i),find(SR==SRPG(s_fieldwind(1,i))),1) =...
        100; % setting percentage to headseas only
    Wind_Beaufort = abs(Beaufort-s_wind);
    Wind_Beaufort1 = Beaufort(1,find(Wind_Beaufort==...
        min(abs(Beaufort-s_wind))));
    Phillygott_we(s_fieldwind(1,i),find(SR==SRPG(s_fieldwind(1,i))),2) =...
        Wind_Beaufort1; %setting the wind in beaufort
    end
    for i=1:35 %35 choosen due to calc in line 180
        %(Beaufort(1,Phillygott_we(i,j,2)+1)) and max Beaufort is 34
        Beaufort(1,i) = Wind_Beaufort1; % Doing a new beaufort,
        %as the old one will not work
    end
end

    load Rothong.mat

%Array created for calculating the correct relative angles

        %Due to trigonimical problems, we have to calculate the angle

        if ( -1/2*pi <= SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,Place_we(i,1))) &   ((SRPG(1,i)-...
                SRA(1,Place_we(i,1))) < 0) | (0 < (SRPG(1,i)-...

                <= 1/2*pi)
            Angleship_we(i,j) = abs(SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,Place_we(i,1)));
            %using abs in order to differentiate the head wind from
            %the tailwind
        elseif ( -pi < SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,Place_we(i,1)) &...
                SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,Place_we(i,1)) < -1/2*pi ) |...
                (  1/2*pi < SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,Place_we(i,1)) & SRPG(1,i)-...
                SRA(1,Place_we(i,1)) < pi )
                Angleship_we(i,j)= - (pi - abs(SRPG(1,i)-...
                    SRA(1,Place_we(i,1))));
               Angleship_we(i,j)= -abs(Angleship_we(i,j));
               %This trick indicates, that the waves are coming from the aft
        elseif SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,Place_we(i,1))== pi
                Angleship_we(i,j)= -(SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,Place_we(i,1)));
                %this takes care of following sea
        elseif SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,Place_we(i,1))== -pi
                Angleship_we(i,j)= -(SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,Place_we(i,1)));
                %this takes care of following sea
        elseif SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,Place_we(i,1))== 0
                Angleship_we(i,j)= abs(SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,Place_we(i,1)));
                %this takes care of head seas
        elseif SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,Place_we(i,1))== 5/4*pi
                Angleship_we(i,j)= (pi - abs(SRPG(1,i)-...
                    SRA(1,Place_we(i,1))));

                %ship-heading above pi

%Calculating block coefficient
CB = Disp/(Lpp*B*T);
%Calculating wetted surface area using empirical formulau from
%Grundlæggende skibs og offshoreteknik

%Using empirical formulau from Grundlæggende skibs og offshoreteknik
%formula 15.13 p. 316

    for j=1:8

CF_ew(i,j) = 0.075/(log10(Re_ew(i,j)-2)^2);
    end
end

    for j=1:8

Fr_ew(i,j) = Vs_ew(i,j)/sqrt(g*Lpp);
    end
end

       delta_bulb_ew = -0.2;
   elseif 0.30 <= Fr_ew(i,j) & Fr_ew(i,j) < 0.33
       delta_bulb_ew = -0.2;
   elseif 0.33 <= Fr_ew(i,j)
       delta_bulb_ew = -0.2;
   end
end
%------------END OF BULB CORRECTIONS------------------
==PAGE:362==
10
%----------END OF RESIDUARY CORRECTIONS------
E_we(i,j) = (A0 + 1.5 * Fr_we(i,j).^(1.8) + A1 * Fr_we(i,j).^(N1))*...
    (0.98+(2.5/(M-2)^4));
G_we(i,j) = (B1 * B2)/B3_we(i,j);
H_we(i,j) = exp(80*(Fr_we(i,j)-(0.04+0.59*Cp)-0.015*(M-5)));
K_we(i,j) = 180*Fr_we(i,j).^(3.7)*exp(20*Cp-16);
CR_we(i,j) = (E_we(i,j) + G_we(i,j) + H_we(i,j) + K_we(i,j) + delta_BT +...
    delta_LCB_we(i,j) + delta_hullform + delta_bulb_we)/1000; %Dividing with 1000 
E_ew(i,j) = (A0 + 1.5 * Fr_ew(i,j).^(1.8) + A1 * Fr_ew(i,j).^(N1))*...
    (0.98+(2.5/(M-2)^4));
G_ew(i,j) = (B1 * B2)/B3_ew(i,j);
H_ew(i,j) = exp(80*(Fr_ew(i,j)-(0.04+0.59*Cp)-0.015*(M-5)));
K_ew(i,j) = 180*Fr_ew(i,j).^(3.7)*exp(20*Cp-16);
CR_ew(i,j) = (E_ew(i,j) + G_ew(i,j) + H_ew(i,j) + K_ew(i,j) + delta_BT +...
    delta_LCB_ew(i,j) + delta_hullform + delta_bulb_ew)/1000; %Dividing with 1000 
    end
end
%Calculating the total resistance from 4.2.13 in Resistance and Propulsion
%of Ships
for i=1:FPG
    for j=1:8
Rf_we(i,j) = real((CF_we(i,j)+CR_we(i,j)) * 0.5 * rhowater15 *...
    Vs_we(i,j)^2 * WSA) ;
Rf_ew(i,j) = real((CF_ew(i,j)+CR_ew(i,j)) * 0.5 * rhowater15 *...
    Vs_ew(i,j)^2 * WSA) ;
    end
end
for i=1:FPG
    Rf_we_final(i,1) = Rf_we(i,find(SR==(SRPG(1,i))))/1000;

% figure()
% plot(nL_non,Rw_tot_non(:,:,1))
% hold all
% plot(lambda(1,:)./Lpp,Sp_we(:,1,1))
% title('Wavespectrum vs. Added resistance')

Rw_180_25_antinon = (Rw_180_25_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
Rw_135_25_antinon = (Rw_135_25_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
Rw_90_25_antinon  = (Rw_90_25_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
Rw_180_20_antinon = (Rw_180_20_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
Rw_135_20_antinon = (Rw_135_20_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
Rw_90_20_antinon  = (Rw_90_20_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
Rw_180_15_antinon = (Rw_180_15_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
Rw_135_15_antinon = (Rw_135_15_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
Rw_90_15_antinon  = (Rw_90_15_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
Rw_180_10_antinon = (Rw_180_10_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
Rw_135_10_antinon = (Rw_135_10_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
Rw_90_10_antinon  = (Rw_90_10_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
Rw_180_5_antinon  = (Rw_180_5_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
Rw_135_5_antinon  = (Rw_135_5_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
Rw_90_5_antinon   = (Rw_90_5_non.*(rhowater15*g*b^2))./Lpp;
nL_antinon = sqrt((nL_non*(Lpp/(2*pi*g))).^-1)';
Rw_tot_antinon(:,:,1) = [Rw_90_25_antinon Rw_135_25_antinon...
    Rw_180_25_antinon Rw_135_25_antinon Rw_90_25_antinon]';
Rw_tot_antinon(:,:,2) = [Rw_90_20_antinon Rw_135_20_antinon...
    Rw_180_20_antinon Rw_135_20_antinon Rw_90_20_antinon]';
Rw_tot_antinon(:,:,3) = [Rw_90_15_antinon Rw_135_15_antinon...
    Rw_180_15_antinon Rw_135_15_antinon Rw_90_15_antinon]';
Rw_tot_antinon(:,:,4) = [Rw_90_10_antinon Rw_135_10_antinon...
==PAGE:375==
12
    Rw_180_10_antinon Rw_135_10_antinon Rw_90_10_antinon]';
Rw_tot_antinon(:,:,5) = [Rw_90_5_antinon Rw_135_5_antinon...
    Rw_180_5_antinon Rw_135_5_antinon Rw_90_5_antinon]';
for i=1:FPG
    for j=1:8
        %Due to trigonimical problems, we have to calculate the angle
        %between the ships heading and the wind
        if ( -1/2*pi <= SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j)) &   ((SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j)) < 0)...
                | (0 < (SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j))) &( SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j) <= 1/2*pi)
            Angleship_we(i,j) =  abs(SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j));
            %using abs in order to differentiate the head wind from the tailwind
        elseif ( -pi < SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j) & SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j) < -1/2*pi )...
                |(  1/2*pi < SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j) & SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j) < pi )
                Angleship_we(i,j)= - (pi - abs(SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j)));
                Angleship_we(i,j)= -abs(Angleship_we(i,j));
                %This trick indicates, that the waves are coming from the aft
        elseif SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j)== pi
                Angleship_we(i,j)= -(SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j));
                %this takes care of following sea
        elseif SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j)== -pi
                Angleship_we(i,j)= -(SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j));
                %this takes care of following sea
        elseif SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j)== 0
                Angleship_we(i,j) =  abs(SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j));
                %this takes care of head seas
        elseif SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j) ==5/4*pi
                Angleship_we(i,j)= (pi - abs(SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j)));
                %this takes care of the specail case of ship-heading above pi
        elseif SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j) ==-5/4*pi
                Angleship_we(i,j)= (pi - abs(SRPG(1,i)-SRA(1,j)));
                %this takes care of the specail case of ship-heading above pi
        end
    end
end
%--------HACKING OF ANGLESHIP_WE and ANGLESHIP_EW---------------------------------
% This hack is made so it fits the only 5 directions from Iship.
% The code itself with Angleship_we is prepared for 8 directions eg.
% following sea, but this has not been incorporated yet. If it is
% incorporated this section has to be disabled.
for i=1:FPG
    Angleship_we(i,1) = 1/4*pi;
    Angleship_we(i,2) = 2/4*pi;
    Angleship_we(i,3) = 0;
    Angleship_we(i,4) = 2/4*pi;
    Angleship_we(i,5) = 1/4*pi;
end

        %Format on added is (AREA,HEADINGS,SPEEDS,FREQUENCY SPAN)
        %and the structure of the variable is:
        % Rows = Headings (i)

        % Columns = Frequenzy Span (c)
        % Plates = Speeds (j)
        % Cubes = Areas (k)
      for k = 1:FPG
         for i =1:5 %Rows (Headings)
            for c=1:length(Rw_tot_antinon) %Colums
                for j = 1:5 %Plates (According to number of speeds)
                    if (Angleship_we(k,j) == 1/4*pi | Angleship_we(k,j) ==...
                          0 | Angleship_we(k,j) == 2/4*pi) & ( 3 <=...
                        find(SR == SRPG(1,k)) & find(SR == SRPG(1,k)) <= 6)
                            Added_we(i,c,j,k) = (Rw_tot_antinon(i,c,j).*...
 Sp_we(c,find(SR == SRPG(1,k))-3+i,k))*percent_we(k,find(SR == SRPG(1,k)));
              elseif( Angleship_we(k,j) == 1/4*pi | Angleship_we(k,j) ==...
                    0 | Angleship_we(k,j) == 2/4*pi )&...
                  ( find(SR == SRPG(1,k)) < 3 | 6 < find(SR == SRPG(1,k)) )
                            if find(SR == SRPG(1,k)) == 2
                            Sp_we(c,1,k) = Sp_we(c,8,k);
                            Sp_we(c,2,k) = Sp_we(c,1,k);
                            Sp_we(c,3,k) = Sp_we(c,2,k);
                            Sp_we(c,4,k) = Sp_we(c,3,k);
                            Sp_we(c,5,k) = Sp_we(c,4,k)
                            Added_we(i,c,j,k) = (Rw_tot_antinon(i,c,j).*...
 Sp_we(c,find(SR == SRPG(1,k))-2+i,k))*percent_we(k,find(SR == SRPG(1,k)));
                            elseif find(SR == SRPG(1,k)) == 1
                            Sp_we(c,1,k) = Sp_we(c,7,k);

                            Sp_we(c,4,k) = Sp_we(c,2,k);
                            Sp_we(c,5,k) = Sp_we(c,3,k);
                            Added_we(i,c,j,k) = (Rw_tot_antinon(i,c,j).*...
 Sp_we(c,find(SR == SRPG(1,k))-1+i,k))*percent_we(k,find(SR == SRPG(1,k)));
                            elseif find(SR == SRPG(1,k)) == 8
                            Sp_we(c,1,k) = Sp_we(c,6,k);
                            Sp_we(c,2,k) = Sp_we(c,7,k);
                            Sp_we(c,3,k) = Sp_we(c,8,k);

                            Sp_we(c,5,k) = Sp_we(c,2,k);
                            Added_we(i,c,j,k) = (Rw_tot_antinon(i,c,j).*...
 Sp_we(c,find(SR == SRPG(1,k))-8+i,k))*percent_we(k,find(SR == SRPG(1,k)));
                            elseif find(SR == SRPG(1,k)) == 7
                            Sp_we(c,1,k) = Sp_we(c,5,k);
                            Sp_we(c,2,k) = Sp_we(c,6,k);
                            Sp_we(c,3,k) = Sp_we(c,7,k);
                            Sp_we(c,4,k) = Sp_we(c,8,k);
                            Sp_we(c,5,k) = Sp_we(c,1,k);
                            Added_we(i,c,j,k) = (Rw_tot_antinon(i,c,j).*...
 Sp_we(c,find(SR == SRPG(1,k))-7+i,k))*percent_we(k,find(SR == SRPG(1,k)));

                            end
                    elseif (Angleship_we(k,j) > 2/4*pi...
                            | Angleship_we(k,j) < 0)
                            Added_we(i,c,j,k) = 0;

%------------------------------------------------------------------------
%Gothenburg - Philadelphia data
for i=1:FPG
    for j=1:8
        %Due to trigonimical problems, we have to calculate the angle
        %between the ships heading and the wind
        if ( -1/2*pi <= -SRGP(1,i)+SRA(1,j)) &   (-(SRGP(1,i)+SRA(1,j)) <...
         0) | (0 < -(SRGP(1,i)+SRA(1,j))) &( -SRGP(1,i)+SRA(1,j) <= 1/2*pi)
            Angleship_ew(i,j) = abs(SRGP(1,i)-SRA(1,j));
       %using abs in order to differentiate the head wind from the tailwind
        elseif ( -pi < -SRGP(1,i)+SRA(1,j) & -SRGP(1,i)+SRA(1,j) < -1/2*pi )...
                |(  1/2*pi < -SRGP(1,i)+SRA(1,j) & -SRGP(1,i)+SRA(1,j) < pi )
                Angleship_ew(i,j)= - (pi - abs(SRGP(1,i)-SRA(1,j)));
                Angleship_ew(i,j)= -abs(Angleship_ew(i,j));
                %This trick indicates, that the waves are coming from the aft
        elseif -SRGP(1,i)+SRA(1,j)== pi
                Angleship_ew(i,j)= (SRGP(1,i)-SRA(1,j));
                %this takes care of following sea
        elseif -SRGP(1,i)+SRA(1,j)== 0
                Angleship_ew(i,j) =  abs(SRGP(1,i)-SRA(1,j));
                %this takes care of head seas
        elseif -SRGP(1,i)+SRA(1,j) == (5/4*pi);
                Angleship_ew(i,j)= (pi - abs(SRGP(1,i)-SRA(1,j)));
                %this takes care of the specail case of ship-heading above pi
        elseif -SRGP(1,i)+SRA(1,j) == (6/4*pi)
                Angleship_ew(i,j)= abs(SRGP(1,i)-SRA(1,j)) - pi;
                %this takes care of the specail case of ship-heading above pi
        elseif -SRGP(1,i)+SRA(1,j) == (7/4*pi)
                Angleship_ew(i,j)= 2*pi-abs(SRGP(1,i)-SRA(1,j));
                %this takes care of the specail case of ship-heading above pi
         end
    end
end

    Angleship_ew(i,1) = 1/4*pi;
    Angleship_ew(i,2) = 2/4*pi;
    Angleship_ew(i,3) = 0;
    Angleship_ew(i,4) = 2/4*pi;
    Angleship_ew(i,5) = 1/4*pi;
end

    AeA0*z-.00142121*PD(i)^6*AeA0*z...
         -0.00383637.*J(c)*AeA0^2*z+0.0126803*PD(i)^2*AeA0^2*z...
         -0.00318278.*J(c).^2*PD(i)^3*AeA0^2*z+0.00334268*PD(i)^6*...
         AeA0^2*z;
Kq5(i,c)   =Kq4(i,c) - 0.00183491*PD(i)*z^2.*J(c);
Kq6(i,c)   =Kq5(i,c) + 0.000112451.*J(c).^3*PD(i)^2*z^2-0.0000297228.*...
    J(c).^3*PD(i)^6*z^2+...
          0.000269551.*J(c)*AeA0*z^2;
Kq7(i,c)   =Kq6(i,c) + 0.00083265.*J(c).^2*AeA0*z^2+0.00155334*PD(i)^2*...
    AeA0*z^2+...
          0.000302683*PD(i)^6*AeA0*z^2-0.0001843*AeA0^2*z^2;
Kq8(i,c)   =Kq7(i,c) - 0.000425399*PD(i)^3*AeA0^2*z^2+0.0000869243.*...
    J(c).^3*PD(i)^3*AeA0^2*z^2-...
          0.0004659*PD(i)^6*AeA0^2*z^2+0.0000554194.*J(c)*...
          PD(i)^6*AeA0^2*z^2;
Kq9(i,c)   =Kq8(i,c) - 0.000591412+0.00696898*PD(i)-0.0000666654*...
    z*PD(i)^6+0.0160818*AeA0*AeA0...
        -0.000938091.*Lrn(i,c).*PD(i)-0.00059593.*Lrn(i,c).*...
        PD(i)*PD(i)+0.0000782099.*Lrn(i,c).^2*PD(i)*PD(i)...
        +0.0000052199.*Lrn(i,c)*z*AeA0.*J(c).*J(c);
Kq10(i,c)  =Kq9(i,c) - 0.00000088528.*Lrn(i,c).^2*z*AeA0*PD(i).*J(c)+...
    0.0000230171.*Lrn(i,c)*z*PD(i)^6-...

function [Joinedcost_field_we,Joinedcost_time_we,Joinedcost_field_ew,...
    Joinedcost_time_ew] = Costs_module(Crew,Shipprice,BSFC_result_we,...
    BSFC_result_ew,lubrication_oil,Prop_we,Prop_ew,speed,time,ship,...
    sel_engine);
%Edit out when running
%----------------------------------------
%Crew = 12;
%Shipprice = 100000000; %Price of the new ship with everything in $US
%---------------------------------------
lub_oil = 1000/100; % Price of lubrication oil in dollars pr. liter.
%See litterature folder for reference. Gulf Oil.
bunker_oil = 620; %US dollars pr. metric ton. 27062014
MD_oil = 899/1000; %US dollars pr. metric kg. 01072014
MD_oil_density = 0.9260; %Taken from TORM data.
dollar = 5.5; % Currency rate for US dollars to Danish krone 27062014
High_salary = 40; %Salary in dollars pr hour
Low_salary = 20; %Salary in dollars pr hour
Food = 7; %Food in dollars pr man pr day
Misc_main = 13; %Misc. cost pr man pr day
Mainengine_main = 43; % Costs in dollars pr. hour in operation no
%matter the load. Data from MAN Diesel
Salaryfactor = (1 / (30 * 24)) * 160; %Estimating 30 workdays month
%and only been paied for 160 hours a month
for i = 1:length(Prop_we(6,:,:))
    for j = 1:length(speed)
% Engine consumption
Engine_fuelcosts_field_we(j,i) = BSFC_result_we(j,i)*bunker_oil +...
    lub_oil * lubrication_oil * time(1,j); %Fuel costs without maintenance
% costs pr field
Engine_fuelcosts_time_we(j,i) = (BSFC_result_we(j,i)/time(1,j))*...
    bunker_oil + lub_oil * lubrication_oil ; %Fuel costs without
% maintenance costs pr hour
Engine_fuelcosts_field_ew(j,i) = BSFC_result_ew(j,i)*bunker_oil +...
    lub_oil * lubrication_oil * time(1,j); %Fuel costs without
%maintenance costs pr field
Engine_fuelcosts_time_ew(j,i) = (BSFC_result_ew(j,i)/time(1,j))*...
    bunker_oil + lub_oil * lubrication_oil ; %Fuel costs without
%maintenance costs pr hour
%Misc. costs
%Aux. engines runs at 77 kg/h in average. Very small peaks. See data from
%Torm
Engine_auxfuel_field_we(j,i) = MD_oil  * 77 * time(1,j);
Engine_auxfuel_time_we(j,i) = MD_oil  * 77;
Engine_auxfuel_field_ew(j,i) = MD_oil * 77 * time(1,j);
Engine_auxfuel_time_ew(j,i) = MD_oil * 77;
